See other formats 


This is a digital copy of a book that was preserved for generations on library shelves before it was carefully scanned by Google as part of a project 
to make the world's books discoverable online. 

It has survived long enough for the copyright to expire and the book to enter the public domain. A public domain book is one that was never subject 
to copyright or whose legal copyright term has expired. Whether a book is in the public domain may vary country to country. Public domain books 
are our gateways to the past, representing a wealth of history, culture and knowledge that's often difficult to discover. 

Marks, notations and other marginalia present in the original volume will appear in this file - a reminder of this book's long journey from the 
publisher to a library and finally to you. 

Usage guidelines 

Google is proud to partner with libraries to digitize public domain materials and make them widely accessible. Public domain books belong to the 
public and we are merely their custodians. Nevertheless, this work is expensive, so in order to keep providing this resource, we have taken steps to 
prevent abuse by commercial parties, including placing technical restrictions on automated querying. 

We also ask that you: 

+ Make non-commercial use of the files We designed Google Book Search for use by individuals, and we request that you use these files for 
personal, non-commercial purposes. 

+ Refrain from automated querying Do not send automated queries of any sort to Google's system: If you are conducting research on machine 
translation, optical character recognition or other areas where access to a large amount of text is helpful, please contact us. We encourage the 
use of public domain materials for these purposes and may be able to help. 

+ Maintain attribution The Google "watermark" you see on each file is essential for informing people about this project and helping them find 
additional materials through Google Book Search. Please do not remove it. 

+ Keep it legal Whatever your use, remember that you are responsible for ensuring that what you are doing is legal. Do not assume that just 
because we believe a book is in the public domain for users in the United States, that the work is also in the public domain for users in other 
countries. Whether a book is still in copyright varies from country to country, and we can't offer guidance on whether any specific use of 
any specific book is allowed. Please do not assume that a book's appearance in Google Book Search means it can be used in any manner 
anywhere in the world. Copyright infringement liability can be quite severe. 

About Google Book Search 

Google's mission is to organize the world's information and to make it universally accessible and useful. Google Book Search helps readers 
discover the world's books while helping authors and publishers reach new audiences. You can search through the full text of this book on the web

LAWS OF LAWN TENNIS- 



THE COURT. 

I. The Court is 78 feet long and 27 feet wide. It is 
divided across the middle by a net, the ends of which are 
attached to two posts, A and B, standing 3 feet outside 
of the court on either side. Tlie height of the net is 3 feet 
6 inches at the posts, and 3 feet in the middle. At each end 




of the court, parallel with the net, and 39 feet from it, are 
drawn the base lines D E and F C, the ends of which are 
connected by the side lines D F and E C. Halfway 
between the side lines, and parallel with them, is drawn the 
half court line I H , dividing the space on each side of the 
net into two equal parts, the right and left courts. On each 
side of the net, at a distance of 21 feet from it, and parallel 
with it, are drawn the service lines K L and M N. 



See back pages of Guide for diagram of single and double 
coait 

11 



12 Laws of Lawn Tennis 

THE BALLS. 

S. The Balls shall measure not less than 2^ inches, nor 
more than 2 9-16 inches in diameter; and shall weigh not 
less than 1 15-16 ounces, nor more than 2 ounces. 

THE GAME. 

3. The choice of sides, and the right to serve in the first 
game, shall bejecided by toss; provided that, if the winner 
of the toss choose the right to serve, the other player shall 
have choice of sides, and vice versa, or the winner of the toss 
may insist upon a choice by his opponent. If one player 
choose the court, the other may elect not to serve. 

4. The players shall stand on opposite sides of the net; 
the palyer who first delivers the ball shall be called the 
server, and the other the striker-out. 

5. At the end of the first game the striker-out shall 
become server, and the server shall become striker-out; and 
so on alternately in all the subsequent games of the set and 
following sets. 

FOOT FAULT RULE. 

Law 6. The server shall before commencing to serve stand with 
both feet at rest behind (i.e., further from the net than) the base-line and 
within the limits of the imaginary continuation of the half -court and side- 
lines, and thereafter the server shall not run, walk, hop or jump before 
the service has been delivered, but the server inav raise one foot from 
(and, if desired, replace it on) the ground, provided that both feet are 
kept behind the base line until the service has been delivered. 

|f 

Official Interpretation of law 6. If a foot be lifted anxl replaced, there 

must be no change of position that can possibly be considered a step. 

IjAW 7. ‚Äî ^The service shall be delivered from the right and left courts 
alternately, beginning from the right in every game, even though odds be 
given or owed, and the ball served shall drop within the service-line, half 
court-line and side-line of the court which is diagonally opposite to that 
from which it was served or upon any such line. 

Law 8. ‚Äî It is a fault if the server commit any breach of Law 7, or if 
the service be delivered from the wrong court, or if the ball served drop in 
the net or beyond the service line, or if it drop out of court or in the 
wrong court. If the server in attempting to serve, miss the ball alto- 
gether, it does not count a fault; but if the ball be touched, no matter 
how sUghtly, by the racket, a service is thereby delivered, and the laws 
governing the service at once apply. 



Laws of Lawn Tennis. 13 

¬ª. It is a Fault if the ball served drop in the net, or be- 
yond the service line, or out of court, or in the wrong court; 
or if the server do not stand as directed by law 6. If the 
server, in attempting to serve, miss the ball altogether, it does 
not count a fault, but if the ball be touched, no matter how 
slightly, by the racket, a service is thereby delivered, and the 
laws governing the service at once apply. 

9. A fault cannot be taken. 

10. After a fault the server shall serve again from the 
same court from which he served that fault, unless it was a 
fault because he served from the wrong court. 

11. A fault cannot be claimed after the next service is 
delivered. 

12. The server shall not serve till the striker-out is ready. 
If the latter attempt to return the service, he shall be deemed 
ready. 

13. A service or fault delivered when the striker-out is 
not ready counts for nothing. 

14. The service shall not be volleyed, that is, taken, be- 
fore it has touched the ground. 

15. A ball is in play on leaving the server's racket, except 
as provided for in law 8, and remains in play till the stroke 
is decided. 

16. It is a good return, although the ball touch the net; 
hut a service, otherwise good, which touches the net shall 
count for nothing. 

17. The server wins a stroke if the striker-out volley the 
service, or if he fail to return the service or the ball in play ; 
or if he return the service or the ball in play so that it drops 
outside of his opponent's court; or if he otherwise lose a 
stroke, as provided by law 20. 

18. The striker-out wins a stroke if the server serve two 
consecutive faults; or if he fail to return the ball in play; 
or if he return the ball in play so that it drops outside of his 
opponent's court ; or if he otherwise lose a stroke as pro- 
vided by law 2a 



14 Laws of Lawn Tennis. 

19. A ball falling on a line is regarded as falling in the 
court bounded by that line. 

20. Either player loses a stroke if the ball touch him, or 
anything that he wears or carries, except his racket in the 
act of striking; or if he touch the ball with his racket more 
than once; or if he touch the net or any of its supports 
while the ball is in play; or if he volley the ball before it 
has passed the net. 

21. In case a player is obstructed by any accident, not 
within his control, the ball shall be considered a "let" 
But when a permanent fixture of the court is the cause of the 
accident, the point shall be counted. The benches and 
chairs placed around the court shall be considered permanent 
fixtures. If, however, a ball in play strike a permanent fix- 
ture of the court (other than the net or posts) before it 
touches the ground, the point is lost; if after it has touched 
the ground, the point shall be counted. 

22. On either player winning his first stroke, the score is 
called 15 for that player; on eiiher player .>i'inning his sec- 
ond stroke, the score is called 30 for that player; on either 
player winning his third stroke, the score is called 40 for that 
player; and the fourth stroke won by either player is scored 
game for that player, except as below. If both players have 
won three strokes, the score is called (/fuca ; and the next 
stroke won by either player is scored advantage for that 
player. If the same player win the next stroke, he wins the 
game; if he lose the next stroke the score. returns to deuce, 
and so on until one player wins the two strokes immediately 
following the score of deuce, when game is scored for that 
player. 

23. The player who first wins six games wins the set; 
except as below : If both players win five games the score is 
oalled games all; ai^d the next game won by either player is 



Laws of Lawn Tennis. 15 

icored adoantage game for that player. If the same player 
win the next game, he wins the set; if he lose the next game, 
the score returns to games all; and so on, until either player 
wins the two games immediately following the score of game* 
all, when he wins the set. But the committee having charge 
of any tournament may in their discretion modify this rule by 
the omission of advantage sets. 

24. The players shall change sides at the end of the first, 
third and every subsequent alternate game of each set and 
9k the end of each set, unless the number of games in such 
set be even. It shall, however, be open to the players by 
mutual consent and notification to the umpire before the 
opening of the second game of the match to change sides in- 
stead at the end of every set until the odd and concluding 
set, in which they shall change sides at the end of the first, 
third and every subsequent alternate game of such set. 
* 25. In all contests the play shall be continuous from the 
first service till the match be concluded; provided, however, 
that at the end of the third set either player is entitled to a 
rest, which shall not exceed seven minutes; and provided, 
further, that in case of an unavoidable accident, not within 
the control of the contestants, a cessation of play which shall 
not exceed two minutes may be allowed between points; but 
this proviso shall be strictly construed, and the privilege never 
granted for the purpose of allowing a player to recover his 
strength or wind. The referee in his discretion may at any 
time postpone the match on account of darkness or condition 
of the ground or weather. In any case of postponement, the 
previous score shall hold good. Where the play Ivis ceased 
for more than an hour, the player who atthe cessation thereof 

*A11 matches in which women take part in tournamenta held under 
the auspicea of the United States National Lawn Tennis Association 
shaU be the best two in three sets, with a rest not excecdmg seven 
minutes after the second set. 



16 Laws op Lawn Tennis. 

was in the court first chosen shall have the choice of courts 
on the recon^mencement of play. He shall stay in the court 
he chooses for the remainder of the set. The last two sen- 
tences of .this rule do not apply when the players change 
eVery alternate game as provided by law 24, 

26. . If a player serve out of his turn, the umpire, as soon 
as the mistake is discovered, shall direct the player to serve 
who ought to have served. But all strokes scored before 
such disooyery shall be counted. If a game shall have been 
completed before such discovery, then the service in the next 
alternate game shall be delivered by the player who did not 
serve out of his turn, and so on in regular rotation. 

27. The above laws shall apply to the three-handed and 
four-handed games, except as below ; ‚Äî 



the three-handed and four- 
handi:d games. 



M 



LiwB OF Lawn Tennis. 17 

s8. For the three-handed and four-handed garnet the 
court shall be 36 feet in width; 4^ feet inside the side linesy 
and parallel with them, are drawn the service side lines K Ml 
and L N. The service lines are not drawn beyond the point 
at which they meet the service side lines, as shown in the 
diagram. 

29. In the three-banded game, the single player shall 
serve in every alternate game. 

30. In the four-handed game, the pair who have the right 
to serve in the first game sbaU decide which partner shall do 
so; and the opposing pair shall decide in like manner for the 
second game. The partner of the player who served in the 
first game shall serve in the third* and the partner of the 
player who served in the second game shall serve in the fourth, 
and the same order shall be maintained in all the subsequent 
games of the set 

31. At the beginning of the next set, either partner of the 
pair which struck out in the last game of the last set may 
serve; and the same privilege is given to their opponenlf fr\ 
second game of the new set. 

32. The players shall take the service alternately through- 
out the game; a player cannot receive a service delivered to 
his partner; and the order of service and striking out once 
established shall not be altered, nor shall the striker-out 
change cousts to receive the service, till the end of the 
set 

33. It is a fault if the ball served do not drop between 
the service line, half-court line, and service side line of 
the court, diagonally opposite to that from which it was 
served. 

34. it is a fault if the ball served do not drop as provided 
in law 35, or if ik touch the server's partner or anything he 
wears or carries^

RBJQULATIONS 

FOR THB 

MANAGEMENT 

OF 

UWN-TENNIS TOURNAMENTS. 



I.‚Äî At Tournaments held by Qubs belonging to the United 
States National Lawn Tennis Association or by Qubs be- 
longing to Associations which belong to the National Asso- 
ciation or by Associations belonging to said National Asso- 
ciation, the Laws of Lawn-Tennis as adopted for the time 
being by said National Association and the Regulations here- 
inafter contained shall be observed. 

2. ‚Äî ^The Officers of the Qub holding the Tournament shall 
have full power over all details concerning said Tournament, 
but shall be entitled to delegate their power to a Committee. 
In case no special Committee shall be appointed by the Offi* 
cers of the Qub holding the Tournament, the powers and 
functions hereinafter delegated to the Conmiittee shall be 
vested in and performed by the Officers of said Qub. 

3. ‚Äî ^A circular shall be issued by the Committee specifying 
the conditions of the competition, and including namrri of 
Tournament Committee and of the Referee. 

4. ‚Äî ^No cheques, orders for money or cash payments in any 
form shall be given as prizes, and the amount actually paid 
for each prize shall in no case be below the advertised value 
20 



Management of Tournaments. 21 

of the same, if incladed in circular. If any challenge cup ii 
offered it shall be deeded to the National Lawn Tennis 
Association. (See form of deed of gift on opposite page.) 
The ^winner of a Challenge Cup shall be entitled to the pos* 
session of the cup until one month previous to the next com- 
petition for the cup, possession being conditional on the giving 
of a bond by the winner satisfactory to the Gub. Should 
the Oub give possession without such bond, it shall be liable 
to the National Association for the value of the cup. 

5. ‚Äî ^The Committee shall elect a Referee, with power to 
appoint a substitute to be approved by them. The Referee 
or his duly appointed substitute must be present at all times 
when play is in progress. 

6. ‚Äî ^The Referee or such other member or members of the 
Committee as may be selected for the purpose, shall have 
power to appoint Umpires and Linesmen, to assign courts 
and to start matches, and the Referee shall decide any point 
of law which an Umpire may profess himself unable to de* 
cide, or which may be referred to him on appeal from the 
decision of an Umpire, and such decision shall be final. 

7. ‚Äî ^The Referee shall, during the Tournament, be ex-offido 
a member of the Committee. 

8. ‚Äî ^The Committee shall help to keep order on the ground^ 
and shall consult and decide upon any question arising out 
of the competition, if summoned for that purpose by the 
Referee or by any two of their number; and they shall have 
power, when so convened, the misconduct of a Competitor 
having been reported to them by a member of the Committee 
or an Umpire, to disqualify the offender, and further to order 
him off the ground, should his misconduct appear to them to 
justify such action, but before such action shall be taken, 
an opportunity of offering an explanation shall be afforded 



^ Management of Toubnamentb. 

to the competitor, whose misconduct has been reported to 
them. When the whole of the Committee of a Club or even a 
quorum thereof may not be available, the members of the 
Committee that are available shall have power to act in cases 
left to the Committee of the Club.

10. ‚Äî It IS the duty of the Linesman to call faults a^d to 
decide strokes relating to the line or lines to which he is 
assigned and to said line or lines only, and such deciaon 
shall be final. Should the Linesman be unable to give a de- 
cision, the Umpire shall decide or shall direct the stroke to 
be played again. 

1 1. The decision of an Umpire shall be final upon every 
question of fact, and no competitor may appeal from it; but 
if an Umpire be in doubt as to a point of law, or if a com- 
petitor appeal against his decision on such a point, the 
Umpire shall submit it to the Referee whose decision shall 
be final. 

NoTB-^A question oi fact relates to events that actually happened. 
A question of law is the construction and application of the laws of 
the game to the admitted facts. 

12. The Referee shall not bet on a match nor shall an 
Umpire or Linesman on the match to which he is assigned. 
An infraction of this rule shall be followed by the inmiediate 
removal of the offending Referee, Umpire or Linesman on 
the complaint of any competitor. 

13. No Competitor may transfer his entry to another 
player. 

14. ‚Äî Competitors shall have a right, by themselves or their 
deputies, to be present at the draw. 

15. ‚Äî ^The draw shall be conducted in the following man* 
ner : Each Competitor's name shall be written on a separate 
card or paper, and these shall be placed in a bowl or hat, 
drawn out one by one at random, and copied on a list in the 
order in whicli they have been drawn.*

C a s e a asid Decisions. 



By James Dwight, 

^'HE following Cases and Decisions are intended to meet 
Vk' questions often asked at Tournaments, and also to 
cover points apparently not provided for in the Laws. 

I. A player standing outside the court volleys the ball or 
catches it in his hand, and claims the stroke because the ball 
was certainly going out of court. 

Decision. ‚Äî He loses the stroke. It makes no difference 
where he was standing. The return is presumed good until 
it strikes the ground outside of the court. 

II. A player is struck by the ball served before it has 
touched the ground, he being outside of the service court. 
How does it count? 

Decision. ‚Äî The player struck loses the stroke. The 
service is presumably good until it strikes in the wrong court* 
A player cannot take the decision upon himself by stopping 
the ball. If it is going to be a fault, he has only to get out 
of the way. 

III. The service is delivered before the striker-out is 
ready. He tnes to return it and fails. Is he entitled to 
have it played over again? 

Decision. ‚Äî No. If he attempts to return the service he 
is deemed ready. 

IV. The striker-out calls "Not ready," for a second 
service. The ball strikes beyond the service line, and the 



36 



Cabie¬ª and Decisions 37 

striker>ou'. claims that the fact that he was not ready makes 
no difference, since a fault cannot be returned, and, there* 
fore, that two faults have been served. 

Decision, ‚Äî The second service goes for nothing. A 
player cannot call ** Not ready," and then have the service 
count, or not, as suits his interests. 

V. A ball having been played over the net bounds back 
into the court from which it came. The player reaches over 
the net and plays it before it falls. Has he a right to do so? 

Decision, ‚Äî Yes, provided he does not touch the net. He 
has a right to play the ball at any time from the moment it 
crosses the net into his court until it touches the ground a 
second time. 

VI. A ball is played into the net; the player on the 
other side, thinking that the ball is coming over, strikes at it 
and hits the net. Who loses the stroke? 

Decision, ‚Äî It is simply a question of fact for the umpire 
to decide. If the player touched the net while the ball was 
still in play he loses the stroke. 

VII Can a player follow a ball over the net with his 
racket, provided that he hits the ball on his own side of the 
net? 

Decision. ‚Äî Yes, the only restrictions are that he shall not 
vcUey the ball until it has crossed the net, and that be shall 
not touch the net or any of its supports. 

VIII. A player's racket slips out of his hand and flies 
into the net. Does he lose the stroke for hitting the net? 

Decision, ‚Äî Yes, if the ball be still in play. It does not 
matter if the racket be in a player's hand or not. 

IX. A player's racket leaves his hand, but meets tlie ba& 
and returns it over the net. Is it a good return ? 

Decision, ‚Äî Yes. There is no law requiring a racket to 



S8 Ciisia AMD DEcmaam 

be m a player's hand when the ball is returned. It woold 
unquestionably be a good return if the racket were held 
against the ground by a player's feet, and the ball bounded 
back off of it. 

X. A single match is played with a double net and mside 
posts. A player touches the net beyond the inside posts, 
and claims that he does not lose the stroke because there 
should be no net more than three feet outside of the court. 

Decision, ‚Äî He loses the stroke. The net where he 
touches it is part of the supports of the net. He might, per- 
haps, have objected to the arrangement of the net before the ' 
match. 

XI. A player returns the ball, and, finding that he can- 
not stop himself before reaching the net, jumps over it. Is 
it a good return ? 

Decision. ‚Äî Law 4 requires that " the players shall stand 
on opposite sides of the net," and therefore the player invad- 
ing his opponet's court loses the stroke. 

XII. A ball passes outside the post of the net and strike^ 
in court. Is it a good return. 

Decision, ‚Äî Yes. 

XIII. A ball going out of court hits the top of the poit 
of the net and bounds into the opposite court 

Decision. ‚Äî It is a good return. 

XIV. The service or the ball in play strikes a ball lying 
in the court. Can it be returned? 

Decision, ‚Äî Yes, if it be clear to the umpire that the right 
ball is returned. 

XV. The server claims that the striker-out must stand in 
the court Is this necessary? 

Decision, ‚Äî No. The striker-out can stand wherever bt 
please* on his own side of the net 



Cases and Decisions 39 

XVI. A bystander gets in the way of a player who fa3tH> 
return the ball. May he then claim a let? 

Decision. ‚Äî Yes, if, in the umpire's opinion, he was pre- 
vented by an accident beyond his control. For instance, if 
the ropes or the seats are allowed to be so near to the court 
that a player is interfered with by them, the stroke should 
not be played again, because the ropes and seats form part 
of the arrangements of the ground. If, however, a spectator 
passes in front of those seats, or places a chair nearer than 
the original line, and so interferes with a player, the stroke 
should be played again. 

XVII. A player is interfered with as above, and the um- 
pire directs the stroke to be played again. The server had 
previously served a fault. He claims the right to two 
services. 

Decision. ‚Äî The fault stands. A let does not annul a 
previous fault. 

XVIII. A return hits the umpire, or his chair or stand* 
the player claims that the ball was going into court. 

Decision',, ‚Äî Stroke is lost. 

XIX. A player receiving fifteen, serves frcm the left 
court. His opponent claims a fault. 

Decision. ‚Äî It is a fault. The service starts from the 
right court under all circumstances. 

XX. At fifteen-all the server by mistake serves irom the 
left court, he wins the stroke and serves again (a fault), 
the mistake is then discovered. Is he entitled to the pre- 
vious stroke? From which court should he serve next? 

Decision, ‚Äî The previous stroke stands. A fault cannot 
be claimed after the next service, good or not. is delivered. 
The next service should be from the left court, the score 
beinjC thirty-fifteen, and the server has served one fault. 



40 Caseb and Decisionb 

XXI. A player serves from the wrong court, he loses the 
stroke, and then claims that it was a fault 

Decision, ‚Äî If Ibe stroke was played in his first service it 
is simply a fault, but if he serves twice into, the wrong court 
he has served two faults, and lost the stroke. 

XXII. The score is five games all, and the umpire di- 
rects the players to play an advantage set. The advantage 
game has been won, when it is discovered that no advantage 
sets are to be played. What is to be done? 

Decision, ‚Äî The set is won at the* eleventh game. It is 
no part of the umpire's duty to decide on the conditions of 
the matches. 

XXIII. A player serves. He hears the umpire call, but 
cannot hear what he says. He knows that the only two 
things that the umpire should call are " fault" and ** let,'' 
and that in neither case can the ball be in play. He, there- 
fore, does not return it, only to find that the umpire has 
called " play." Has he any redress? 

Decision, ‚Äî No. 

XXIV. The umpire calls ¬´ fault," and then instantly 
changes and says ** play." The striker-out fails to return the 
ball, and he claims he was prevented by the umpire, and 
also that the umpire cannot change his decision. 

Decision. ‚Äî The umpire should call a let and the service 
be taken again. 

XXV. A ball drops near a line, the player appeals, and 
the umpire calb <*play." The player misunderstands the 
call and lets the ball fall. He then claims to have the stroke 
played again. 

Decision. ‚Äî The stroke stands. 

XXVI.‚Äî The ball strikes the ground close to a line th¬´ 
scorer scores the stroke against the striker. On appeal to tlw 



Cases and Decisions. 41 

linesman, the latter decided that the ball was not ouu 
Which decision stands? 

Decision, ‚Äî The scorer has no right to consider a ball out 
until the linesman has called to that effect, therefore, the 
decision of the latter must be accepted. The decision of a 
linesman affecting his own line is final. 

XXVII. A return strikes the cord running along the bot- 
tom of the net and bounds over. Is it a good return? 

Decision. ‚Äî Yes. 

XXVIII. During play a ball is thrown into court, and 
the ball in play strikes it or a player steps on it. May a let 
be claimed ? 

Decision. ‚Äî Yes. 

XXIX. The server's first service strikes his partner. 
Does he lose the stroke or is it a fault? 

Decision. ‚Äî A fault. 

XXX. A player serves a fault and it is then discovered 
that it is his partner's service. Does the fault stand ? 

Decision, ‚Äî Yes. 

XXXI. In a four-handed competition one player does 
not appear in time to play, and his partner claims to be 
allowed to play single-handed against the opposing pail 
May he do so? 

Decision, ‚Äî No. 



THE SEASON OF 1908. 



PIE season has been a very interesting one, though its finish wa 
disappointing. 

It is needless to dwell on the increase of tournaments, etc. Neve 
in this CQuntry has lawn tennis been so popular. 

The Davis Cup matches have added a great deal to our own events 
Very early in the spring it was thought settM that an English team would 
come to this country to play the preliminary in Philadelphia, but after 
long delay it decided not to come. Later, just as we were planning to 
play England in Melbourne it was decided to send the team here and 
Longwood was selected as the place. 

The interest shown was a certain proof of the popularity of the game. 
For the Championship there were 149 entries, by far the largest number 
that there has ever been. The ranking committee has ranked more 
names than ever before. Everything goes to show the growth of the 
game. 

In looking over the country one finds that Lamed is unquestionably 
the best player we have. He played at Longwood and at Newport and 
against Parke and Ritchie. All these matches he won and with some- 
thing to spare. After him come Wright, Alexander, Clothier and Little 
in the East. In Chicago and the Middle West Emerson stands first 
In the South Thornton seems easily first. He won the Southern Cham- 
pionship, the Gulf States and Tennessee. He lost to Touchard in 
the Championship after a hard match. On Che Pacific Coast, Long 
seems to be first with McLaughlin a very close second. Then came 
Gardner and Bundy. It is possible to get a little better idea of the 
standards of play in the different parts of the country this year, than it 
has been before. For instance Long beat Johnson and Niles. Emer- 
son reached the Semi-Final at Newport, where he lost to B. C. Wright. 

Thornton is in about the same class as Touchard.

The Championship 45 

The score gives little idea of the match. Clothier plaved a very strong 
game, and a great deal of the time he played as well or better than 
Alexander, but when the critical times came Alexander seemed always 
able to let out a link and go ahead. 

The match between Wright and Emerson was closer than had been 
expected. Wright won the first set easily, but either he slowed up a 
Ut&e or Emerson eame on, or probably a little of both, for Enierson 
won the second set 6-4 and reached 5-4 in the third set. It was his last 
effort and he lost the next three games and the set 7-5, and the fourth 
set 6-3, He played very well a^inst a strong player. 

The weather was too bad on Wednesday and Thursday for play. 

On Friday the conditions were fairly good, but it seemed as if tlie 
waiting had not suited Alexander. He was in nothing like the form that 
he showed against Little. Wright played very well, but the match was 
a disappointment The service won for four games and then Alexander 
got no more till the score was 5-2 against him, and then but one 6-3. 

The second set went easily to Wright, 2-0, ^-1, 4-2, 6-3. Alexander 
won the first and second games of me third set, but afterwards he 
was hardly in it, and again lost 6-3. 

The Championship was played in beautiful weather and there was a 
ve^ large gallery. 

Wright won the first game on his service to 15. Lamed won the three 
next games to 15, the fourth to love; the sixth after deuce had been 
called once and the last to 15. He passed Wright easily when he tried 
to come up or occasionally lobbed over his head. In the second set 
the play was more even for a time. Larned won the first game and 
Wright the second and tlurd, showing rather better form than in the 
first set, but he could get no farther and only one game reached deuce. 
The last set was really dose, but Lamed always seemed to have a little 
the advantage. Wright won two games and then Lamed three. Then 
each won his service till the score was 6-all; Lamed each time having 
won the vantage game. He won it again and then Wright's service, 8-6. 
At times the match was very brilliant, but at no time was Wright better 
than Lamed and most of the time much inferior. Thus ended the larg- 
est tournament that has been held for our Championship. Fortunately 
the weather was fine for the first four days, or it would have been im- 
possible to carry it through in any reasonable time. 




HAROLD H. HACKETT and FREDERICK B. ALEXANDER 

Champions in Doubles of the United States 



NATIONAL DOUBLES CHAMPIONSHIP. 

WRIGHT and Little had won tlie Eastern Doubles at Longwood and 
beaten the other winners at Bay Ridge, and now met the 
Champions of last year, Alexander and Hackett. There was some veiy 
pretty play, but the Champions were clearly a stronger team than their 
opponents. Hackett began serving and won a love game. Alexander 
and Hackett won the second and third games as well. In all of the 
sets Wright and Little did a good deal of lobbing and it was either 
short or went out. In the foiui;h game they played better and won it at 
deuce. The next three went to the Champions and the set 6-1, and 
29 strokes to 13. The second set was very different, being hard and 
evenly fought all the way through. It ran along with the service winning 
till the score was 3-all, when Wright and Little lost the service, 4-3; 
Hackett won his, 5-3. Wright and Little made it 5-4 and then won on 
Alexander's service, 5-all. Alexander took the vantage game almost 
wholly on his opponents errors, and then Hackett again won his service 
after deuce had been called three times. 

The third and last set needs little description. In the fourth game 
deuce was called once and the other five games Wright and Little won 
only four strokes. It was a very brilliant exhibition on the part of Alex- 
ander and Hackett. 

The Scores: 6-1, 7-5,^2^ 

THE DOUBLES CHAMPIONSHIP. 
East vs. West and West vs. South. 
npHE Double preHminary matches between the Eastern, Western 
‚ñ† and Southern Double teams to see who would prove the winner 
to play the National Champions, Hackett and Alexander at Newport 
the following week for the Clmmpionship of the country, took place 
on the fine grass courts of the Crescent Athletic Club, Bay Kidge, N. Y., 
August 13, 14 and 15. 

'Phe drawing brought the East vs. South match first, the winners 
to play the West. The results were as follows: 
East vs. South. 
Wright and Little (Ejist.) vs. Whitehead and Winston (South). Tlie 
matches proved rather ontv-sided. 'I'he latter seemed to be nervous 
and over anxious, which no doubt prevented them from playing their 
best game, while the former team played strong all-around tennis which 
gave them the match in three straight sets, 6-0, 6-3, 6-2. 
This brought the East and W^est pairs to face each other. 

East vs. West. 

The Western team proved themselves much stronger than the South 

though they were also defeated in three straight sets. They played good 

tennis and at times during the match put the Easterners to their liest 

game to defeat them, which they did by the following score, 7-5, 6-3, 6-4.

THE MATCHES FOR THE DAVIS CUP. 



AMERICA VS. BRITISH ISLES. 



WDOTH Englg,nd and America challenged Australia for the Cup. It 
- "-^ was finally agreed that the preliminary should be played at the 
Liongwood Cricket Club grounds, Boston, Mass., where two fine grass 
courts were prepared for the event The American team consisted of 
Alexander, Hackett, Lamed and Wright. It was known at the time 
that only Alexander and Wright could go to Australia, but as the regu- 
lations allow the use of different teams for the different sets of matches, it 
seemed wise to name the full strength of the country. 

The play began on Thursday, September 17 at Longwood with the 
match between Lamed and Parke. There had been some disappoint- 
ment, when the British team was named, as neither of the Dohertys or 
Smith were on it, but the result showed that this country needed its best 
men to win. 

Lamed vs. Parke. 

Parke is very tall, strong and active. His service has little or no twist, 
but it is fast and well placed, and not easy to do much with. He seems 
to prefer the back of the court, and to be better on his passing strokes 
backhanded than " forehanded. He also can smash a short lob as it 
should be done. At times he was unsteady, but at other times his 
strokes across court were beautifully exact, and extremely fast. He lost 
because he was not quite as good as Lamed. 

Lamed on his part played the match with great judgment. He found 
soon that he was the better and could win without taking too much risk 
in nmning up, and he was contented to go along slowly and to take no 
chances. Occasionally,, as in the last part of the third set he would 
suddenly begin playing a much faster game, and when he did so, he 
won easily.

68 International Cup Matches op 1908 

Wright vs. Ritchie. 

In the meantime the other Singles Match was going quite the other 
way. Wright was in very poor form and though he carried the first 
game to deuce and won the second, Ritchie ran out the set 6-1, in a 
very few minutes. He played for the mast part in the back of the court 
and kept a good length and good direction. 

The second set looked more hopeful for a time. Wright won the 
first game to love, lost the second after deuce had been called onoe, 
won the third, and after losing the next two, brought the score even at 
3-all. He made but four strokes in the rest of the set In the third set 
Ritchie lost the first and third games, but won the set 6-¬£. Wright 
worked very hard to save the match at Uie end, but he was too uncertain 
to hold his own against Ritchie's steadiness. Thus the first day aided 
with the score even, each country winning a match. 

The Scores: 



Ritchie 

Wright 

Ritchie 

Wright 

Ritchie 

Wright 

Friday was again a beautiful day and there was a larger gallery than 
on Thursday, about 2,500 people being present. They saw a match 
such as is seldom seen in its wonderful brilliancy and imcertainly. 
Against so good a pair as Alexander and Hackett Ritchie's careful, 
well-placed strokes are not so effective as in Singles. Parke, on the other 
hand, showed a game for which he had not been given credit His 
speed and placing and his ability to get anything and to return it not 
only fast but well-placed, showed how great a double player he would be, 
if he and his partner had played enough together to make such an accu- 
rately working machine as Alexander and Hackett.

Lamed vs. Ritchie. 

The weather on the last day was again very fine, and the conditions 
good for play. Lamed started very badly, apparently being nervous. 
Occasionally he made a fine stroke, but in most cases the ball went out 
or into the net. In this way five games went to Ritchie who was playing 
steadily and well, but who was not pressed to any great extent. With 
Ihe score five love against him Lamed started playing in earnest. He 
brought the score to 4-5, but" he lost the next game and the set 6-4. 
However, he had not got going and though he lost the first game of the 
second set, he took the next with the loss of three strokes only. Ritchie 
won the fifth game to love and the seventh at deuce, but Lamed won 
the others, only one going to deuce and the second set 6-3. He ran out 
the third set very fast. He lost the fifth and sixth games, but of the 
others only one reached deuce. Ritchie's game did not seem to have 
fallen off, but Lamed was clearly the stronger. The fourth set started 
with four long games, three of which Ritchie took. Lamed won the 
next after deuce had been called twice, and then ran the set out with 
the loss of but four points. After learned once got going he was un- 
mistakably the stronger player of the two. Ritchie played a good, 
steady game and the result was no reflection on his play.

Wright vs. Packe. 

This match is harder to describe properly for the play varied from 
very good to very poor. The first set ran evenly to S-alfand then Wright 
won two games 5-3, one at deuce and one to love. * Parke won the next 
two, losing only one stroke, 5-all. Then the advantage game went twice 
to Wright and once to Parke, and at 8-all Parke quite out-played Wright 
and won both the next games at love. The second set was shorter. 
Parke won the first two games, lost the third and fourth won the fifth and 
sixth. Then Wright won his last game in the set to 30 as Parke took 
the last two; one being at deuce, two sets love. The third set was 2- 
all and 4-2 for Parke. The next game Wright won at deuce and fol- 
lowed it with three more in which Parke got only three points. The 
fourth set was the deciding one of the matdi. Wright won the second, 
third and fourth games abo the sixth 4-2. Parke made a great effort 
and won three games one to 30 and two to 15; 5-4 for Parke. Wright 
rose to the occasion and ran the set out It was Parke's last real suc- 
cess, though he won the third and sixth games of the fifth set. Wright 
deserves credit for pulling out the match when it seemed to be lost.

AMERICA VS. AUSTRALASIA DAVIS CUP MATCHES. 

On October 9 Wright (Captain) and Alexander sailed from Van- 
couver for Sydney, .N. S. W. The trip was a long one and the players 
took moderate exercise such as throwing the medicine ball, cricket 
and skipping rope. On their arrival at Brisbane, Sydney, and Mel- 
bourne, they were met by the various tennis committees and given a 
royal welcome. At both Brisbane and Sydney they were urged to stop 
over and play, but as there was only a short time to get into condition, 
th^ began training at once in Melbourne. 

The Warehouseman grounds where the matches were to be played 
were not quite in readiness, so for the first ten days private courts 
were put at their disposal and the leading Australian and American 
flayers had many good Single and Double practice matches together. 
Brookes practiced on his own private court with Wilding who was 
living wim him. 

The Victorian Championships began the week before the Internationals 
and all the leading players participated. Brookes and Wright defaulted, 
Wright the day before he met Wilding as he thought it best to put all 
the strain on the Final contest Alexander, however, played through, 
meeting and losing to Wilding in the Finals. In the Doubles both 
International pairs met, Brookes and Wilding winning. 

Summer was fast approaching as it does in South Australia and the 
temperature would range from 135 degrees to 150 degrees in the sun, 
consequently the players had to be careful and not overdo. The matches 
were scheduled for the 26th, 27th and 28th of November, but the first 
day it rained. 

Alexander vs. Brookes. 

The 27th was a beautiful day, and when Brookes and Alexander 
walked on the court at 2 p. m. the three stands and dub house porch 
were crowded with enthusiastic Australians, although there were a few 
Americans who were most loyaL 

Alexander lost the first four games by being erratic, but soon settled 
down and only allowed Brookes one more game that set which was a 
great surprise not only to the world's Champion but to dose followers 
of the game. Both players were winning on tneir favorite shots, Brookes 
on his service and slmrp volleying, and Alexander by his beautiful 
ground strokes and smasning. Not until 7-all was called did Brookes 
break through winning by ^7. In the third set Alexander continued 
with the same brilliancy, but Brookes missed several easy volleys and 
only got two games losmg 6-2. With two sets to one in nis favor and 
winning the last set so easily, it seemed as though Alexander would 
win, but he lost his aggressiveness, while Brookes began to force matters 
and won the last two sets though not easily as 3-all was called in the fifth.

told on Wilding as he lost his sting, thus allowing Wright to be the aggres- 
sor on all occasions winning the last 3 sets, 7-5, 6-3, 6-1. In Uie Le^t set 
Wilding played almost entirely a back court game.

THE DOUBLES. 
Wright and Alexander vs. Brookes and Wilding. 

The splendid weather conditions still prevailed and on Saturday 
afternoon when the Doubles started, every possible seat was occupied 
and many people were refused admittance. 

The four players entered at the same time and the gallery stood up 
and cheereci. This naturally enthused the players, although they 
realized this match would, without doubt, decide the eptire contest. 
There was no choice between the teams as all four had reputations as 
Doubles players. Brookes and Wilding having won the All England 
Championship the previous year and Alexander and Wright both being 
U. S. National Champions, Alexander for two years with Hackett (and 
present Champions) and Wrio^ht for three years undefeated with Ward, 
rassed records and general opinion proved true as Brooks and Wilding 
won, but only after a hard and exciting 5-set match. Both sides were 
aggressive, and it was a battle to work through the service. Wilding 
was steady all through, but not brilliant. He seldom failed to return 
the service close to the net thus making it hard to volley. Brookes 
served and volleyed well as usual, was weak on his back-hand, but pro- 
tected this by running around the ball. He fell off badly in the fourth 
set, but came back brilliantly in the fifth, and saved the day. Wright 
served strongly and led on aces. His smashing was deadly and his 
ground strokes sure, but not brilliant. He played poorest in the third 
set. Alexander was brilliant all through. His smashing was better 
than any of the others, but he never lobbed thus allowing his opponents 
to phy close to the net. Excitement was intense and applause plentiful 
until tne Americans led 2-0 in Uie final set and their service, then there 
came a lull. It looked like a victory and the Cup, but unfortunately 
Alexander lost his service twice in the last set and the match was won by 
Australasia, 6-4, 6-4, 5-7, 1-6, 6-4.

International Dinner. 

The International dinner was planned for Saturday night and could 
not be postponed. It was a great success as over fifty sat down. Many 
speeches were made and the Americans were presented with most at- 
tractive opal pins, the Australian emblem, while the Americans presented 
Mr. Chomley, the referee, with a silver case. Many speeches were noade 
and each speaker remarked that it was greatly appreciated by all that 
America sent a team such a distance to Iryand regain the Ciip. They 
also remarked about the American's sportsmanship. Sunday-was spent 
quietly as all the players were glad for the rest. 

Wright vs. Brookes. 

The first match Monday was the Brookes vs. Wright contest, and it 
was a contest. The players had met on three previous occasions, WVight 
winning twice in a five-set match in the London Championships and 
in a four-set match in the International a week after. Wright was at 
top form and the same year he won the U. S. Championship. Two years 
later, however, Brookes won three straight winning the Lnglish Cham- 
pionship the same year. 

The stands were packed when the players entered the court at two'' 
o'clock, and were heartily cheered. At the time the temperature was 
145¬∞ in the dazzling sun. Brookes wore a hat the entire match, while 
Wright went bare headed. 

Brookes started off brilliantly getting many aces on first services, 
and after receiving Wright's service would follow it in, and a battle at 
the net would follow, Brookes usually winning. At four love Wright 
saw that there was little chance of winning the set, and if he did in such 



International Cup Matches of 1908 79 

heat he would have but little left for the balance of the match, so very 
wisely he lobbed as he did to Wilding, but did not play any short ones 
as Brookes volleyed too well. Brookes won the next two games and 
set six love, but the tossing made them long ones and took some of his 
snap away. Wright continued these tactics until finally Brookes gave 
up following in on Wright's service. It was here the match changed 
and the players were on an even footing. It was a match of skill with the 
racket, headwork and endurance. Brookes won the second set 6-3, 
and after exchanging services until 4rall was called and Brookes had 
40-30 with his service next, it looked as though the match was over and 
the Cup lost. Wright won the game by three brilliant shots and allowed 
Brookes but one more game, winning 7-5. There was not enough 
time to change flannels between the sets as the Australian rules only 
allow three minutes, so the players took advantage of this precious time 
by baUiing their heads with cold wet towels, whidi was most refreshing. 
Two sets to one for Brookes sounded like a safe lead, but Wright's 
tactics in the early part of the match caused Brookes to lose his severe 
service and forcing tactics, and in the foiuiJi set Wright simply took all 
chances and rushed matters winning 6-2, making but few errors. This 
continued until Wright was 4-1, when Brookes revived and the score was 
called 4-all amid cheers. 

A battle of services and the survival of the fittest followed. Both 
players were working mechanically, the real tennis had gone. They 
could only reach the middle of the court to volley and the spectators to 
both players seemed to be a blur. Brookes had 7-6, 8-7 ana 10-9, when 
Wrignt put a most determined look on his face and won the last three 
games 12 points to 6. The onlookers yelled bravo to both players imtil 
* they reached the clubhouse after a match that lasted two hours and 
forty minutes.

Wilding vs. Alexander. 

When W^ildinf? and Alexander went into the court it was ten minutes 
to five, and another lon<y match would have caused a play-off the fol- 
lowing day as both captains agreed to stop play each at six. 

Both plavers appeared to be a trifle nervous as was only natural with 
the future nome of the Cup depending on this match. After the pre- 
liminary warminor up, Wilding began serring and won the game, also 
the next two. This gave Wilding a commanding lead which he held 
until the end winning by 3 sets to love and without losing a single service 
game. Alexander could not seem to get started, while Wilding took 
all chances making but few mistakes and being very aggressive. After 
the match he was carried off the court on the shoulders of his friends. 

The Americans received every possible courtesy during their visit, 
and the committee did all in their power to assist the challengers. The 
court was in perfect condition, an<l great pains were taken in selecting 
the linesmen who were mostly leading players. It is only hoped that 
America will be able to send another team to Australia this year. It 
can be truly said that they play the game' for sport's sake.

THE WOMEN'S NATIONAL CHAMPIONSHIP. 



I HE National Championship for Women's Singles and Doubles and 
* Mixed Doubles was again held at AA'issahickon, the home of the 
Philadelphia Cricket Club, w^here all of these historic events have been 
held since the inception of National Championships for the fair sex. 
A record-breaking entry list both in size and quality greeted the onlookers 
and made the hard-working committee hustle to get everything nm off 
on time. 

A successful innovation was the playing of matches in the mornings 
as well as the afternoons and the change seemed to be acceptable to all 
concerned^ 

The severe heat was responsible for tw^o or three defaults in the First 
Round and also accounts for the retirement of Miss Kutroff, who after 
a hard set with Miss Johnson of the Merion Cricket Club of Philadelphia, 
which Miss Johnson won 7-5, succumbed to the heat and h¬´ad to retire 
after the first two games of the second set had been played. 

One noteworthy match in the First Round was the defeat of Miss C. T. 
Chase, a former Pennsylvania State Champion, by Miss Matilda Borden 
of .the home club.. The balance of the matclies in the First Round were 
won without much difficulty by the favorites. 

Miss Borden in the Second Round defeated Miss Curtis of Boston, 2-6, 
6-3, 6-3. 

Mrs. Pouch, who as Miss Helwig, won the National Championship 
some few years ago, defeated Miss Ema Marcus quite easily by the 
score of 6-3, 6-1. Mrs. Barger-Wallach played her usulal steady game, 
defeating Miss C. Scott, the Chami)ion of the Country Club, West 
Chester (N. Y.), in straight sets. 

Getting down" to the Third Round, Miss Borden, the local player, kept 
up her wonderfully good work and in straight sets defeated Mrs. A. G. 
Miles. 

The Third Round was productive of good tennis all through and 
brought together in the Fourtli Round Miss Rotch who defeated Mrs. 
Pouch after a hard match. 

JVIrs. Barger-Wallach caused the downfall of the local player, de- 
feating Miss Borden, 6-3, 6-2. This being really the first time that Miss 
Borden had gone through such an imporUmt tournament, the strain 
naturally told on her, and it speaks well tliat the game is producing such 
a good quality of players who j)romisc to make strong bids for the Cham- 
pionship in me future. 

Miss Johnson of Staten Island defeated Miss Cohen of Philadelphia 
and Miss W^agner caused the downfall of Miss Necly of Chicago. This 

83 



84 Women's National Championship 

was an excellent match, each winning a set 6-4 and 5-all, being called 
in the final set when Miss Wagner's steady and accurate play won the 
remaining two ^mes giving her the set, 7-5. 

In the Semi5'inal Round Mrs. Barger-Wallach, still playing her 
steady game from the base-line, relying almost entirely on her ability 
to place the ball, defeated Miss Rotch, 6-2, 6-4. The score, however, 
does not indicate the closeness of the match. 

Miss Wagner accounted for Miss Johnson. 

The Final Match brought together Mrs. Barger-Wallach and Miss 
Wagner, opponents of entirely different styles of tennis, created a great 
deal of interest. Miss Wagner's forcing tactics won her the first set 
6-4, but nothing daunted, Mrs. Wallach came back strong in the second 
set, winning handily by the score 6-1, and then app¬ªarently stronger in 
the last set run out the game 6-3, giving her the priwlege of challenging 
Miss Evelyn Sears. 

The Championship Round was played in front of the grand stand 
before a large crowd of enthusiastic spectators. Both players clung to 
the base-line game, rarely attempting to go to the net and they both 
relied on their placing and not on their service, and the rallies were long 
and sustained, the ball frequently going over the net in single rallies 
15 to 20 times. 

The first set went to Mrs. Barger-Wallach, score 6-3, and the second 
was nip and tuck until 5-all was called, when Miss Sears ran out the 
necessary two games obtaining the set. After the usual rest the third and 
final set was started and some long sustained rallies were evidenced, 
and the score crept to 5-all, 6-all, 7-all, until the wonderfully ac- 
curate placing of Mrs. Wallach began to tell and she ran out the two 
remaining games, winning the matc¬£ and Championship, 9-7. 

The Ladies' Doubles brought together in the Final Round Miss 
Sears and Miss Curtis of Boston who defeated Miss Neely and Miss 
Steever of Chicago, 6-3, 5-7, 9-7. The match was marked by fast tennis 
with close net work on the part of the Western pair and the smashes 
and cross court shots added greatly to the interest and gameness of the 
contest 

Miss Rotch paired with Niles in the Final Round of the Mixed 
Doubles and defeated Miss Hammond and Little, the National 
player. Niles' curious twist service was used with deadly ^ect, and 
both of the men displayed great ability in their hard smashing and 
lobbying. 

In the Men's Singles Beals Wright won, defeating Dr. Dewhurst, 
5-7, 6-2, 3-6, 8-6, 6-3. 

Taking all together the tournament was the most successful of all the 
National tournaments held, and the sustained interest shown not only 
by the contestants, but by the spectators indicates a more marked activity 
in the game among the fair sex than ever before. 

The tournament was ably handled by Mr. Patton as chairman, who 




MRS. BARGER-WALLACH 

Lady Champion of the United States

was most energetic and worked hard for the interests of all concerned. 

One of the features not mentioned before was the fact that the rooms 
of the Chestnut Hill Academy, formerly the Wissahickon Inn, were 
secured for the players, which proved a great convenience as the building 
is practically at the entrance of the Philadelphia Cricket Club groimds.

THE LONQWOOD TOURNAMENT. 



npHE eighteenth open annual tournament of the Longwood Club 
* and the Eastern Doubles took place on the grass courts of the club, 
Boston, Mass., July 27 and following days: 

^ Favored with the finest kind of weather for tennis and a large entry 
list which included all the high ranked placers of the country, caused 
many well played and exciting matches aurmg the week which brought 
out daily an interested and enthusiastic crowd to witness the different 
events, particularly the match between Beals Wright and F. B. Alex- 
ander, both players showing fine tennis and will be long remembered. 
The tournament was won by R. D. Little, which entitle! him to play 
the holder of the bowl, W. A. Lamed, who retained the same.

EASTERN DOUBLES. 



TpHE Eastern Ojpen Doubles, as usual, was played on the same courts 
* and during tne same week as the Ijongwood Club Singles. The 
entry list was a large one and contained many strong teams. The two 
teams looked upon as the strongest and expected to go through the 
tournament were Beats Wright and R. I). Little and W. A. Lamed and 
Greorge L. Wrenn, and such was the case. After both teams having 
played many interesting matches they met in the Finals and had an 
exciting five-set match. Wright and Ijittle won, which entitled them 
to the right of playing the winners of the South vs. West match, to be 
held the week of August 10 on the courts of the Crescent Athletic Club, 
Bay Ridge, N. J.

MIDDLE STATES CHAMPIONSHIP. 



TpEIE Middle States tournament is one of the oldest events now held 
^ ' in the country. It was started in the early days of the game back 
in 1885, and has always been one of the best tournaments held during 
the tennis season. 

The tournament as in past years, took place on the fine grass courts 
of the Orange Lawn Tennis Club, South Orange, N. J. 

The Singles were won by E. P. Lamed, the Champion *s young brother, 
while the Doubles went to the credit of W. A. Lamed and George L. 
Wrenn.

LAWN TENNIS IN THE SOUTH. 



CHAMPIONSHIP OF THE SOUTH. 



TpHE tournament for Championship of the South was held for the third 
* time on the beautiful dut courts of the Atlanta Athletic Club in 
the week of Jul^ 6 to 11. Seventy-seven players, representing Georgia, 
Tennessee, Louisiana, Florida, North Carolina, New York, Indif^a, 
Illinois and Virginia showed the wide interest that the Southern is 
attracting, and also indicated an advance both in numbers and grade 



of play. 
While 



de Nat Thornton, who had won the Singles Championship the 
year before, was regarded as a strong probability in defending his title 
successfully, it was seen the first day's play that there were several de- 
cidedly strong contestants for the title, including Cowan Rodgers of 
Knoxville, Whitehead and Winston of Norfolk, Grant and C. Y. Smith 
of Atlanta. 

After many exciting matches being played the honor fell to Hugh 
Whitehead to meet the Champion, Nat Thornton, for the Challenge 
Match. Keen interest in the result brought out a gallery of several 
hundred people, and while local sentiment was strongly for the Atkmta 
player to win. Whitehead had also a host of friends and shared equally 
in the applause from the grand stand. Thornton was quick to take ad- 
vantage of the fact that Whitehead played a back court game, relying 
upon his accuracy in pass-shots; so Inomton took the net from the start. 
There for two sets he played remarkably good tennis, covering the court 
completelv, and displaying a variety in returns, cross-courting, base- 
line smasnes and short strokes, which bewildered his opponent, and won 
for him the first two sets at 6-2, 6-1. By this time Wnitdiead realized 
the situation was desperate and went strongly for the next set, winning 
it at 6-3, and looked to have a good chance for the fourth set also. But 
with the score five-all, he lost his service and Thornton, by winning his 
own took the deciding game and match, thereby becoming for the second 
year Champion of the South. 

The weatner for the week was ideal, clear and not too warm, and the 
toiunament passed without a hitch. For this great credit is due Referee 
Frank Reynolds, who gave constant attention to every detail of the meet.

SOUTHERN DOUBLES CHAMPIONSHIP. 



The U. S. N. L. T. A. Doubles Championship is played in three 
sections ‚Äî Boston. Chicago and Atlanta. The latter was held July 6 
to 11 at the Atlanta Athletic Club and brought together twenty-jseven 
teams representing seven states. Much regret was felt that owing to 
the illness of Charles Rodgers, the Rodgers Brothers* team of Knoxville 
was broken up. But Cowan Rodgers and Farrell of Nashivlle teamed 
up¬ª and for a time looked as if they might reach the Finals, but were 
disposed of in the Third Round by Whitehead and Winston. Carter 
ana Middlebrooks, Southern Intercollegiate Champions, were beaten 
in the same round by Sanders and McGinnis of New Orleans. The 
suprise of the tournament was in the strength of the two young players. 
Cowan and Van Gilder of Knoxville. Although it was their first real 
tournament experience, they reached the Semi-Finals without much 
trouble, defeating several very good teams, and being beaten by White- 
head and Winston in straight sets after a very pretty contest, losing the 
last set at 7-5. 

Smith and Howell of Atlanta, who had made a good showing for 
several years came out to the Finals without the loss of a set Here 
they met Winston and Whitehead, and after a rather brilliant start, and 
winning the first set at 6-4, proved unequal to the contest, and lost three 
sets at 0-6, 2-6, 3-6. 

This victory for Winston and Whitehead entitled them to diallenge 
Grant and Tnomton, two years Southern Champions, for the title, and 
the honor of representing the South in the National East-West-South 
tournament at tne Crescent Athletic Club in August. The weather for 
the matdi was ideal, and the interest among the tennis contingent in- 
tense. A pallery that overflowed the grand stands assembled early, and 
all indications were for a close, struggle. The Norfolk pair, however, 
took the upper hand from the start, Whitehead retummg everything 
with great steadiness, and Winston killing at every opportunity with 
sharp cross-courts or by bouncing the ball over the back stops. While 
Grant plajred with great steadiness and excellent judgment, Thornton 
had a decidedly off-day, driving the ball repeatedly into the net or just 
beyond the base line, and the spectators quickly saw how the match 
would end. In a very short time Winston and Whitehead had annexed 
the first and second sets at 6-2, 6-3; and after losing the third set at 4-6, 
won thhe fourth and deciding set at 6-4.

LADIES' SINGLES SOUTHERN CHAMPIONSHIP. 



The Ladies' Singles, while not a large toiu*nament as yet, showed 
growth in number of entrants, and some very good tennis resulted. 
Much of the interest of the week was centered in the ladies * events, and 
the rivalry between Atlanta and New Orleans, the only two cities rep- 
resented was keen. In the Final Round Miss Gertrude Jones of At- 
lanta and Miss Olga Von Nordenflycht of New Orleans met The 
first set went to Miss Von Nordenflycht, but here she had spent her 
strength in a measure, the score being 6-8. In the second and third 
sets. Miss Jones, with great steadiness and excellent judgment had 
little difficulty in getting a good lead, and was never overtaken, winning 
these two sets at 6-2, 6-2. This placed her in the Challenge Round 
against Mrs. Blanc Monroe of New Orleans, two times winner of the 
Southern Championship. 

Tliis match afforded some very pretty tennis, and the first set was not 
decided until the twelfth game had ended, going to Mrs. Monroe at 7-5. 
In the second set which proved to be the last. Miss Jones made a de- 
termined stand, many of tne games being deuced a number of times, but 
Mrs. Monroe kept b^ ppnrt ppvered at all times, and by well placed 



Southern Championships l(y5 

base-line shots, or frequently coming into the net for effective kills, 
brought the match to a close at 6-4, thereby winning for the third time 
the title and the handsome trophy cup.

SOUTHERN INTERCOLLEGIATE TENNIS ASSOCIATION. 



Officers for 1908-1909. 



President, E. V. Carter Jr. (University of Georg^). 
First Vice-President, Grover C. Middlebrooks (University of Georgia). 
Second Vice-President, Lowndes Connally (Georgia School of Tech- 
nology). 
Secretary and Treasurer, W. M. Poteat (Wake Forest). 

The second annual tournament of the Southern Intercollegiate Tennis 
Association was held on the courts of the Atlanta Athletic Club at 
Atlanta, Gra., on May 11, 12, 13, 1908. Seven colleges located in Georgia, 
North Carolina, Alabama and Mississippi were represented. E. V. 
Carter, Jr. (University of Georgia) was me surprise of the tournament, 
defeating W. M. Poteat (Wake Forest), the 1907 Champion, by the 
score of 6-8, 6-4, 6-4, 6-1, in Singles. 

Carter and Middlebrooks (University of Georgia) defeated Poteat 
and Eajnshaw (Wake Forest), the 1907 Champions in Doubles, in one 
of the hardest fought matches ever played in the South, the score being 
6-1, 3-6, 6-4, 3-6, 13-11. 

Nat Thornton, the Open Southern Champion for 1907 and for 1908, 
refereed the tournament. 

The Southern Intercollegiate Tennis Association was organized by 
the Atlanta Athletic Club and the tournaments are played under its 
auspices. The club has recently completed a $50,000 country club 
annex six miles from Atlanta. An efiort will be made to house all 
the entrants in the Southern Intercollegiate Tennis tournament for 1909 
in this building.

ONE of the most successful tournaments held by the Elmira Tennis 
Club opened Tuesday, August 4, 1908, with an entry list in the 
Men's Singles of 24 players.

of Canandaigua, pitted against O. N. Reynolds of Elmira; G. W. Wyckoff

of Elmira,

McLaughlin won from Dr. Wilbur, 6-3, 7-5.

daigua, N. Y., and McLaughlin, Buffalo, N. Y.; McLaughlin 
winning the tournament by a score of 6-3, 6-1, 6-2, and the right to 
challenge the Cup-holder, Howard Bissell of Buffalo. 

The Challenge Match was played the following day, Mr. Bissell 
retaining the Cup by score of 6-4, 1-6, 6-3, 6-3. 

Cup now bears the names of H. W. Hamlin, Canandaigua, N. Y., 
1905; E. L. Robertson, Syracuse, N. Y., 1906; Howard Bissell. Buffalo, 
N. Y., 1907 and 1908.

WHITE MOUNTAIN CHAMPIONSHIP. 



THE third annual tournament for the Championship of the White 
Moimtains, held on the courts of the Crawford House Tennis Club 
at Crawford Notch, New Hampshire, showed an improvement over 
previous events both in the number of entries and the quality of play 
of past seasons. The week was notable alike for good sport and good 
feUowidiip. 

Irving Wright was the star of the tournament, wresting the Challenge 
Cup from Russ, who had held it two years, and with Dabney for a 
partner, taking the Doubles trophies as well. In the Singles he dis- 
posed of F. W. Cole in the Fourth Roimd, Inman in the Semi-Finals and 
Jones in the Finals. Cole was the only one of the three to get a set, 
but Jones put up an exceedingly good game and each of the three sets 
required extra games. In these matches, as in the Challenge Round, 
Wright was too steady and too resourceful. He adapted himself easily 
to any style of play and was particularly good overhead. 

In Doubles Cole and Inman made a very good impression in their 
early matches and many picked them to win. In the Semi-Final Round 
they met Jones and Henshaw. With the score one set-all, play had 
to be stopped on account of darkness. The next day the match was 
replayed irom the start The Providence pair seemed to have guaged 
their opponents and won easily. 

Wright and Dabney came through to the Finals, defeating Page 
and Bra^ord in the First Round and Russ and Cresson, last year's 
winners, in the Second, both matches being in straight sets. Li the 
Finals they seemed to be off for one set, which they lost at 6-1. There- 
after their play was well-balanced and steady, the features being Wright's 
smashing and Dabney 's passing strokes.

LAWN TENNIS IN THE WEST. 



TENNIS IN AND ABOUT CHICAQO. 



T'^HE tennis season of 1908 was unquestionably marked by a raeat return 
* of public interest in the game throughout the West In Chicago 
this was made noticeable by the necessity of increasing the facilities for 
public play in the city's parks. In fact so great and insistent was this 
demand that the park boards finally met the popular demand by not 
only adding courts but also throwing open the grounds set apart for 
tennis for Sunday play ‚Äî a distinct departure from the traditional policy 
of the park commissioners. In the clubs also the increase in interest 
was felt and in all of them the membership was fully up to the highest 
quoto allowed. The active play among the club members was an es- 
pecially pleasing feature and the club tournaments at all of the dubs 
invariably drew large entry lists. The open tournaments, however, 
failed to bring out play of unusual quaUty and much disappointment 
was felt in the non-appearance during the season of many of tne best of 
the Western players. Kreigh Collins was again out of the game and 
will probably never again participate in open events owing to his eye 
trouble; Heath Byford likewise failed to return to the game, while Ward 
C. Burton, the Minneapolis star, was kept out owing to illness. James 
J. Forstall was away in Europe and Rueben G. Hunt failed to come East 
for the Western season. Carr Neelalso disappointed his Chi^^o ad- 
mirers by allowing his titles, won in 1907, to go by default. Tne ab- 
sence of these men therefore made the tournaments seem unusually 
dull, although all of the veterans and many of the younger players 
improved. 

Of the women the absence of many good players was keenly felt and 
interest in their events consequently fell off sadly. Mrs. Robert Wil- 
liams, the brilliant winner of the Illinois and Western tournaments of 
1907, removed to Boston and was lost to the game owing to family cares. 
So slack was the interest among the women players in fact that the 
W^estem tournaments excepting Singles were declared off and social 
events, without fee, substituted in order to take care of the few women 
that responded to the call for entries. 

118 




J. ALLEN ROSS 
Western Intercollegiate Champion 



1^ Lawn TennIs m The West 

THE WESTERN INTERCOLLEGIATE AND INTERSCHOLASTIC 
TOURNAMENTS. 



‚Ä¢ The first tournaments of importance in the West were the Western 
Intercollegiate and Western Interscholastic tournaments, which were 
played on the courts of the University of Chicago. The first of these 
was played on June 15 and following days. It drew entries from nearly 
all of the leading Western universities and was well attended. The 
courts are ideally situated and are among the finest clay courts in the 
West, the play being, consequentljr of a high order. In both the Singles 
and Doubles events the University of Chicago carried off first honors, 
thus scoring the two points necessary to secure for that institution per- 
manent possession of the trophy. In the Singles J. Allen Ross, the 
former National Interscholastic Champion, made his first appearance in 
intercollegiate company and won the title fairly easily, defeating Muir 
of Minnesota in the Fmal Round, 6-1, 6-4, 10-8, while Ross and Hart 
of Chicago took first place in the Doubles. 



WESTERN INTERSCHOLASTIC CHAMPIONSHIP. 



In the Western Interscholastic Championship held immediately after 
the intercollegiate event, the honors were taken in the Singles by R. 
Shields, representing the Calumet High School, who defeated Byford 
of the University High School after a hard five-set match (6-3, 6-1, 3-6, 
4-6, 6-1) in the Final Round. In the Doubles the Evanston Academy 
represented by K. Bragg and Foster came out victorious. The field was 
unusually large and classy and nearly all of the leading "prep*' schools 
were represented. 

THE OPEN TOURNAMENTS. 



Of the open tournaments only two of importance were played in 
Chicago last year ‚Äî ^the Illinois and Western Championships, The 
Wanderers Cricket and Athletic Club held its usual open event scheduled, 
but it is said that friction between the tennis and cricket enthusiasts was 
responsible for its cancellation. So that with this event removed there 
remained but two open tournaments in the Chicago district In the 
place of the Wanderers' tournament, however, the Onwentsia Club of 
Lake Forest filled in bv scheduUng a Round Robin Invitation Doubles 
event which was played during the week prior to the Western and re- 
sulted in some interesting matches, and won by H. L. Waidner and J. C. 
^eely.

THE CHICAGO TENNIS LEAGUE. 



Officers for 1909. 

GJeorge H. Dm^combe, President 

Lawrence B. Burford, Secretary-Treasurer. 



THE SEASON IN THE CHICAGO TENNIS LEAGUE. 



Among the clubs themselves the chief interest of the season centered 
in the play in the Chicago Tennis League. Five clubs competed in 
the league matches last year ‚Äî six men in the Singles and three teams in 
the Doubles representing each of the dubs in each of the matches. 
The schedule was well arranged and large galleries turned out to see the 
oon tests. The method of counting was altered slightly, each match won 
scoring one point in favor of the victorious dub, where heretofore the 
entire team match was counted as a win or a loss. It was believed that 
this would discourage the defaulting of individual matches and the 
scheme worked out well and will be followed again the coming season. 
The fight in the league was especially severe among the three leading 
clubs and resolved itself into a three-cornered contest between the Ken- 
wood, Aztec and University of Chicago Clubs.

WESTERN CHAMPIONSHIP 



The twenty-first annual Western Championshij> was decided on the 
courts of the Kenwood Country Club of Chicago during the week 
of July 25 under the most favorable conditions, excepting for the oppres- 
sive heat which at times bothered the players. In this event L. Harry 
Waidner ran through the field in the Smgles quite easily, where he met 
Charles S. Peters in the Final Round Peters started out well and took 
the first set, but Waidner came back strong and took the next three in 
succession, winning the match, 4-6, 6-3, 6-2, 6-1. In the Challenge 
Hound Waidner faced Nat Emerson, the holder of the title, and here 
once more history repeated when after the most gruelling, exciting 
match ever seen on a Western court the Cincinnatian was returned the 
wimier, 8-10, 6-0, 3-6, 6-1, 7-5. Several times Waidner had this match 
within a point, the ball "letting'* at one crucial period and saving the 
noatch for Emerson. 

In the Men's Doubles Waidner and Emerson, paired together, won 
out hands down defeating Hayes and Peters by default in the Final Round 
through an unfortimate condition of affairs that prevented the playing 
of the match. 

In the Women's Singles the title was retained by Miss Carrie B. Neely, 
who defeated Miss Miriam Steever (challenger) after one of their famous 
long-drawn out matches ‚Äî ^the final score being, 3-6, 6-4, 6-2. The 
Women's Doubles was won by Miss Neely and Mrs. Barnes, and the 
Mixed Doubles by Miss Steever and Mr. Kicker.

NATIONAL DOUBLES PRELIMINARY. 



With the awarding of the Western Championships to the Onwentsia 
Club for 1909, together with the awarding to the same club of the Pre- 
liminary Round-Robin Doubles, for the right of challenging for the 
National Doubles title at Newport, the season of 1909 is eagerly looked 
forward to in the West and in Chicago especially, and it is thought that 
the game will sustain an even greater boom in popular interest during 
the coming season, as the Western Association has announced its inten- 
tion^ of sparing no means to secure for Chicaf^o the best players from all 
sections of the country. The Preliminary Doubles will be especially 
welcomed to Chicago this year, which will be the first since 1893 that 
the Western enthusiasts have had the opportunity of witnessing an event 
of national importance.

MEXICO CITY TOURNAMENT. 



nr%[E first open lawn tennis tournament for the Championship of the 
^ Republic of Mexico took place April 11 and followmg days on the 
dirt courts of the Mexico Country Club. There was quite an interest 
in the tournament owing to a gooci entry list of visiting ptayers, including 
Claresice Hobart, Irving Wright, George Wagner, Semp Kuss, Chiu*les 
Cresson, A. V. Duncan and a number of Texas pWers who made the 
trip in a special car in charge of the president of the Texas Lawn Tennis 
Association, E. L. Scott 

The playing of the visitors was not up to their reputation owing prin- 
cipally to the bright light which takes time to become accustomed to. 

The playing of Claude Butlin, winner of the Singles, and Harvey and 
Paul MacQuiston (of Mexico City), winners of the Doubles, was very 
strong, they outplaying the visitors all through the tournament. 

Large crowds were present during the week, much interest being shown 
in the different matches, particularly the Finals in Singles and Doubles. 

The tournament was a grand success and the visiting players returned 
home much pleased with the kind treatment they received at the hands 
of the tennis committee and the members of the Country and Univer- 
sity Clubs where they were entertained. 

The Scores:
