ALLEGHENY COUNTY TOURNAMENT 



npHE Edgewood Club of Edgewood Park, Pa. is the Champion team of 
* the Allegheny Tennis Association for the second time m succession 
defeating Pittsburg Field Club in their match on the Field Club team*s 
pounds. Beside the Club Championship, Edgewood has the Junior 
Champion, D. W. Lloyd, who won in a field of thirty-two placers, all 
19 years of age or less; the strongest Double team in the association is 
I>r. T. W. Stephens and P. O. Naly; this team was also runner-up in 
the Western Pennsylvania Doubles Championship; Sunstein, the former 
Princeton man, was runner-up in Singles Championship of Western 
Pennsylvania. 

The Pittsburg Field Club finished second, losing only one match, 
that to the Edgewood Club, Brighton has the Champion of the Novices 
in C. K. Robinson. Shadyside and Electric Clubs finished in the order 
named, the former team being a disappointment, their stars being un- 
able to carry the team to the top. 

The tournaments held by the association were well attended and had 
more entries than ever before, there being 228 in all. Miss Marion 
Aiken won the Women's Singles; Mrs. T. K. Laughlin and Greorge 
D. Edw^ards, the Mixed Doubles; Percy Siverd and C. L. Childs, the 
Men's Doubles and H. J. Kendall the Men's Singles. Kendall had no 
trouble at all in cleaning up the best players that went against him; 
Champion Childs, the Western Pennsylvania tournament winner did 
not play well all week, and those who watched the play closely were not 
surprised at the result. Kendall defeated lams, Siverd, Sunstein and 
Childs in turn. Siverd was the only one who pressed him into a deuce 
set. He was in rare form while here. 

The Scores: 

Men's Singles. 

Preliminary Koimd: Moore beat Condit, 6-2, 3-6, 6-0; Egli beat

CHAMPIONSHIPS OF PACIFIC NORTHWEST. 



'T'HE eighteenth annual tournament for the Championships of the 
‚ñ† Pacific Northwest was held as usual by the Tacoma Lawn Tennis 
Club, Washington August 3 to 8, at Tacoma, "Washington. ^ The entey 
list was as usual quite representative, all the strong players in the juris- 
<iiction being present, with but two exceptions, McBumey of Spokane 
and Schwengers of Victoria. California was represented by Miss Hazel 
notchkiss, Lou R. Freeman, and E. Jordan. The Doubles event was 
exceptionally interesting, and in fact, the only Doubles contest in the 
Pacific Northwest last season. At all the other tournaments the strong 
pairs were so broken up that one pair always had a walk awav. At 
Tacoma there were four pairs in the running: Freeman and Walter A. 
Ooss of Portland, who won the tournament; S. L. Russell and Joe C. 
Tyler, the famous Seattle pair; Payne and Armstrong of Taconia, the 
International Champions, and Jordan and Breeze. In the Singles, 
Freeman beat Tyler in straight sets, from the back court Jordan de- 
feated Gross rather easily, but was defeated by Payne. In the Finals 
Freeman took the first set, 7-5, and Payne the second set, 6-4, when 
Freeman retired Freeman's Singles game is not what it used to be. 
Apparently his health is not the best, and he refuses to run to the net as 
he invariably used to do. 

Miss Hotchkiss had things all her own way in the Ladies' Singles 
and Doubles, but tasted defeat in the Mixed Doubles, the first and only 
time in her Northern trip.

PACIFIC NORTHWEST INTERNATIONAL. 



TpHE Pacific Northwest International Lawn Tennis Association 
* Championship took place the week of August 10, at Everett, Wash., 
and brought togetner an entry list of the best players. 

A good drawing caused many exciting matches to be played which 
kept up the interest throughout the week. 

Frank T. Payne, winner of the Championship in 1906, fought his 
way through one of the halves when he met James C. Tyler in the Finals, 
which was played on Saturday afternoon when a large crowd was present 
to witness me contest, which was won by Tyler after a hard-fou^t five- 
set match and successfully defending the title he won the year before. 

Payne and Armstrong of Tacoma won the International Doubles, 
winnmg their matches with ease. The winners having won the Doubles 
Cups three years in succession became the permanent holders. 

The Everett Club gave the visitors a splendid week of sport, and the 
trophies prescribed were exceptionally handsome. 

The Scores: 

International Singles. 

Preliminary Round: C. J. Hall (Everett) beat D. S. Niles (SeatUe), 
6-4, 6-1, 7-5; J. C. Tyler (Spokane) beat W. E. Bums (Vancouver), 
6-4, 6-2, 6-1; F. T. Payne (Tacoma) beat T. H. Bow den (Everett), 
6-2, 6-2, 6-2. 

First. Round: Albert Armstrong (Tacoma) beat D. T. Bellinger 
(Portland), 6-2, 6-4, 7-5; Tyler beat Hall, 3-6, 7-5, 6-2, 6-4; Payne beat 
W. A. Goss, (Portland) 6-4, 3-6, 8-6, 10-8; B. Rhodes (Vancouver) beat 
Arthur Reniington (Olympia), 6-1, 6-4, 6-4. 

Semi-Fmal Round: lyier beat Armstrong, 6-4, 6-1, 9-7; Payne beat 
Rhodes, 6-1, 8-6, 6-3. 

Final Round: J. C. Tyler beat F. T. Payne, 6-3, 6-4, 2-6, 0-6, 6-1. 

International Doubles.

6-1; Pajnie and Armstrong beat A. E. Barry and M. H. Wildes (Everett),

THE INTERCOLLEGIATE TOURNAMENT. 



TpHE twentv-eighth Intercollegiate lawn tennis tournament took 
* place at the Merion Cricket Club, Haverford, Pa., October 5 and

Nat. W. Niles (Harvard) won the Singles, which made him the In- 
tercollegiate Champion. H. M. Tilden and A. D. Thayer (Pennsyl- 
vania) won the Doubles.

NEW ENGLAND INTERCOLLEGIATE TOURNAMENT. 



TpHE ninth annual tournament of the New England Intercollegiate 
* Lawn Tennis Association was held on the courts of the Longwood 
Cricket Club on the week beginning May 25. It was marked by an 
unusually high standard of play in the Singles, but the Doubles were 
disappointing in form throughout In the Singles, T. K. Thompson of 
Williams and W. C. White of Wesleyan ivere the favorites, but S. B. 
Smith of Dartmouth proved to be the proverbial dark horse, and de- 
feated Thompson in tne Semi-Finals and his team mate, L. F. Stem of 
Dartmouth, m the Finals with ease, not being forced to go a deuce set 
in either case. 

In the Doubles, the teams from Technology and Amherst were the 
favorites, but Technology, after defeating Amherst in the First Round, 
lost to Williams, whose team in turn lost to Wesleyan in the Finals by 
a score of 3 sets to one. The play throughout the Doubles was most 
erratic, and far below that displayed in former years. 

The Scores: 

Men's Singles. 

First Round: W. B. Houlton (Weselyan) beat J. A. Vernon (Brown), 
9-7, 6-4; S. B. Smith (Dartmouth) beat H. H. Bergmin (Trinity), 6-1, 6-1. 

Second Round: L. F. Stem (Dartmouth) beat P. R. Fanning (I'ech- 
nolo^y), 8-6, 6-4; W. W. Peters (Vermont) beat A. W. Bunmouth 
beat S. L. Wolff (Amherst), 6-2, 7-5. 

(Bowdoin), 6-4, 6-0; W. C. White (Wesleyan) beat H. P. Greene (Ver- 
mont), 6-3, 6-3; J. K. Thompson (Williams) beat L. Richmond (Dart- 
mouth), 6-3, 7-5; H. W. Hubbard (Amherst) beat G. P. Hyde (Bowdoin), 
6-4, 7-5; S. B. Smith (Dartmouth) beat J. B. Parker (Technology), 
6-8, 6-4; 6-4; m. L. Oakley (Williams) beat W. B, Holton (Wesleyan), 
6-4, 10-8. 

Third Round: J. K. Thompson (Williams) beat W. C. White 
(Wesleyan), 4-6, 6-3, 6-1; S. F. Stem (Dartmouth) beat W. W. Peters 
(Vermont), 6-8, 6-0, 6-4; R. L. Oakley (Williams) beat W. W. Hubbard 
(Amherst), 6-3, 5-7, 7-5; S. B. Smith (Dartmouth) beat S. L. Wolf 
(Amherst), 6-2, 7-5. 

Semi-Final Round: S. B. Smith (Dartmouth) beat J. K. Thompson

LAWN TENNIS IN CALIFORNIA. 



PACIFIC STATES CHAMPIONSHIP. 



TpHE Pacific States Championship of 1908 commencing the week of 

* September 7 and ending the following week (19th) proved to be 
extra attractive to the lawn tennis players of California owing to the 
visit of the three Eastern players, Irvmg Wright, Nat Niles and Wallace 
Johnson. 

The tournament was held on the asphalt courts at Del Monte. No 
better place for a tournament could be selected as the hotel and surroimd- 
ings are most inviting and fine for a week's outing. 

All the events were successfully run off; many well-played and hard- 
fought matches were contested during the tournament both in Singles 
and Doubles. The Eastern visitors had to battle hard to get as far in 
the tournament as they did, finding the pace set for them by the Califor- 
nia players rather fast as the courts caused quick bounds to the ball and 
somewhat of a different game than is played on grass or dirt, but not 
enough difference to interfere with Eastern players' game. The all 
round play of Long, McLoughUn, Grardner, Bundy and Janes was of 
the best, jjarticularly the two former. 

The ladies are all strong players, those in particular are the Misses 
Sutton, Mrs. Farquhar (formerly Miss Jones and twice a National 
Champion), Misses Hotchkiss, Ryan and Myer. 

The junior players of which there are a number, are most promising 
for the future of the game in California. The two who played in the 
Finals of the Junior department, Robert Strachan and Charles Rogers, 
played the different strokes in good form and used judgment in placing 
the ball. 

The treatment the Eastern men received at the hands of the tourna- 
ment committee and the players was most pleasant and everything 
was done to make their visit a most enjoyable one.

LAWN TENNIS IN SAN FRANCISCO 



Tennis in and around San Francisco has seen a very successful 
season in the year of 1908. It is safe to say that there are more 
devo ees tnan ever before in this vicinity. More clubs are being organ- 
ized^^anta Rosa, Berkeley and Oakland being three very large and 
active tennis organizations started since the first of the year. Golden 
Gate Park is a great tennis center now and all of the best players in 
the city and from about the bay take part in tournaments held on its 
courts. The visit of he Eastern team, Irving Wright, Nat. Niles and 
W. F. Johnson to our Del Monte event has given a great impetus to 
the game and the season of 1909 will be a busy one.

The San Francisco Championship 

Was held during the latter part of May on the courts of the California 
Lawn Tennis Club and although the entry list was small, it included 
all of the best players in the city. M. McLoughlin successfully defended 
his title and obtained his second win on the beautiful Grant trophy, 
which must be won three times. This trophy was won in 1903 by 
J. D. McGavin, 1904 by C. P. Muidock, 1905 by C. R. Gardner, 1906 
by Charles Foley, 1907 by M. E. McLoughlin and once again in 1908 
by the same man. 

G. Janes won from Charles Foley in a hard three-set match, but fell 
before Long's onslaught. This match was also a close one, however, 
and Long was forced to his utmost to take the measure of the little man. 
The score was 3-6. 6-2, 6-4. In the Semi-Finals of the other half C. 
Gardner defeated H. Long in a tremendous three-set match which went 
to fifty games before he was declared the victor. ^ 

M. Long put up a^great game in the Finals with Gardner. He put 
the finishing touches to that which his brother had started in the morn- 
ing and won almost as he pleased. 

The Challenge match was a dandy. Long started off at a great 
pace and was never headed in the first two sets. Notwithstanding, the 

games were all close and the plajdng brilliant McLoughlin seemed a 
it nervous and failed to assume the net as regularly as is his custom, 
lliis may partly have been due to the fact that Long often and more 
than often passed him down the sides and with cross court shots. At 
the beginning of the third set it looked as if Long would surely win, 
but he became careless. McLoughlin, quick to see his advantage, soon 
regained his confidence and changed his tactics. Having won me third 
set 6-3 he came through with a wonderful burst of speed in the fourth 
and dropped but one game. Long had lost his assurance and failed 
to play the same brand of tennis that had characterized his previous 
^lay and in the last set he did well to get the three games which Mc- 
'ighlin dropped in winning the set and match. Swre, 4-6, 4-6, 6-3, 
6-3.

average and the doseness of many of the most important matches kept 
the spectators in a state of expectation and excitement as was evidenced 
by a constantly increasing attendance resulting in a record-breaking

Open Singles. 

There were forty^ix entries in the Open Singles, the most prominent 
of which were A. E. Bell, Southern California Chanq>ion in 1901, 1902 
and 1903, Eugene Overton, Southern Cahfomia Champion in 1904 and 
1905, T. C. Bundy, winner of the Colorado, Long Beach and Venice 
tournaments this year, Charles Rogers, Interscholastic Champicm of 
Southern California. At the outset the wiseacres predicted tnat the 
winner would be returned from among Bundy, Bell, Overton, Browne, 
Sinsabaugh and Wayne, with Bundy and Bell having the best chances. 

This forecast proved to be a good one as Bundy defeated Bell in the 
Finals by a 9Core of 6-1, 6-2, 5-7, 6-1, thus winning the tournament 
and also the Championship, as last year's Champion, Melville H. Long 
of San Francisco, owing to summer school work, did not come down to 
defend his title. 

Bundy proved decisively that he was the best player in the South by 
defeating m turn such men as Harry Rogers, Charles B. Hopper, A. V. 
Duncan, Claude Wayne and A. E. Bell, losing only one set in the tourna- 
ment and winning a total of 83 games as agamst a total of 81 games lost 

His winning me Championship for 1908 proved a most popular 
victory, as he is a thorougn, good sportsman and has been "knocking 
at the door" for several years. 

Ladies' Singles. 
The presence of our redoubtable Champion, Miss Ma^ Sutton, lent 
the greatest interest to this event, as owing to her joumeyings eastward 
and abroad, she has not contested in any Southern Camomia Cham- 
pionship Tourney for several years. As her sister. Miss Florence Sutton, 
won the Championship in 1907, it was therefore necessary for ''Miss 
May" to play through this tournament which, as a matter of course, she 
did, winnmg the tournament and Championship without the loss of a 
set 

Ladies' Singles Special 

In order to encourage new women players who would otherwise not 
enter the tournaments just to be unmercifully beaten by one of ihe 
Suttons, the association several years ago inaugurated an event which 
has proven to be popular among both players and spectators alike. It 
is a special event for ladies barring anyone who has ever won an open 
tournament In this event there were eight entries, the winner bemg 
Miss Mary Browne, who won without the loss of a set Miss Browne 
defeated Miss Laura McVay in one Semi-Final match, while Mjss 
Helene McVay won from Miss Fanny Rowan in the other Semi-Final

Junior Singles. 

Eleven entries in the Junior Singles brought out some promising 
youngsters, some of whom are already making our first-class veterans 
"step some." Ward Dawson, Winired Mace and Charles Rogers 
were thought to have the best chance to win the much-coveted first 
prize offered by the association, a trip including all emenses to the Pacific 
States Championship tournament at Del Monte, Cal, in September, 
to play in the Junior event as Southern California's representative, 
which was won by Rogers 

Gentlemen's Doubles. 

From the first, the sixteen teams entered in Men*s Doubles promised 
many snappy matches, with perhaps the Rogers Brothers, Bundy and 
Overton, Wayne and Variel, or Bell and Way looking the likeUest 
bidders for mial honors. However, even that prospect fell short of 
what the outcome proved. Many exciting matches were played before 
Bell and Way won the tournament, which entitled them to meet the 
Champions, Sinsabaugh and Brown. This proved to be a hard-fought 
and exciting five-set match which was won by the Champions by the 
score of 10-8, 4-6, 6-4, 2-6. 6-4. 

Mixed Doubles. 

In Southern California, perhaps, more than any other locality. Mixed 
Doubles is a most important factor in the tennis tournaments. Probably 
the presence of the Sutton sisters accounts for this unusual interest, 
for their games almost qualifies them to a place among the men. For 
that reason this Final gave promise of a sensation, two Suttons con- 
testing, paired with two of the best players in the South. But the match 
was not up to par. Both men were greatly fatigued from their Men 's 
Doubles, which match had just preceded this, and the victory went 
slowly but surely to "May" and Sinsabaugh, 6-3, 6-3. 

The tournament was brought to a most successful close Saturday 
evening with a banquet and ball at the magnificent Hotel Virginia, 
during which the prizes were presented to the various successful aspirants. 
The tournament was voted by one and all to be the best ever neld in 
Southern California.

THE OJAI VALLEY TOURNAMENT. 



The annual Ojai Valley tournament tooki>lace on the courts at 
Nordhoff, CaL, April 9 and following days. The entry list was a good 
one comprising such leading players in Northern and Southern Cali- 
fornia as McLoughlin, Gardner, Bundy, Sinsabaugh, Freeman, Miss 
May Sutton, Miss Florence Sutton, Mrs. Farquhar, Mrs. Bruce and 
Miss Ryan, which resulted as expected in an interesting tournament 
and many well contested matches being played. There were five events: 
The Men's Singles, which was won by C. R. Gardner, the Men's 
Doubles which were won by McLoughlin and Gardner, Ladies' Open 
Singles won by Miss May Sutton, Ladies' Open Doubles won by Miss 
May Sutton and Miss Ryan, and the Interscholastic event which was 
the attraction of the tournament. Eighteen schools were represented 
by two boys from each and six girls' sdiools were entered in tne young 
ladies 'events. Charles Rogers of Los Angeles won the boy's tourna- 
ment and Miss Ryan of Marlborough School won the girls. 

Great credit is due W. L. Thatcher, of Thatcher School, in the Ojai 
Valley for his management of the events, together with the tournament 
committee he worked faithfully and hard to have things run smoothly. 

A dance at the Foothills Hotel, band concert and reception on the 
courts and the final reception and dance at the Thatcher School went to 
make the social side of the tournament a grand success. 



LAWN TENNIS IN CANADA 



THE CANADIAN CHAMPIONSHIPS. 



A T annual meeting of the Canadian Lawn Tennis Association, held 
-^^ in Toronto in the spring, it was decided to enlarge the scope of the 
association by putting representatives from each province in Canada 
on the general committee, and at the request of the Montreal represen- 
tative the annual tournament was given to Montreal. This tournament 
has always been held at Niagara-on-the-Lake in the past, and it was an 
experiment to see what Montreal could do. The local committee 
A. C. Dimlop and H. M. Suckling, took the matter in hand, 
and on August 1 the biggest tournament ever held in Montreal was 
started on the beautiful grass courts of the Mount Royal, Lawn Tennis 
Club. There were over 150 entries from everywhere in Eastern Canada , 
among them Ross-Foulkes, the Canadian Champion, from Ottawa, 
Bogs from Barrie, McAvity, Stewart and Inches from St. John, N. B., 
Miss Moyes and Miss Summerhayes from Toronto, and several Toronto 
men who entered, but did not show up. T. Y. Sherwell of Montreal 
won all the open events. He played a strong heady j^me through all 
his matches not being really pressed in any. In the Finals of the Open 
Championship he had a hard fight with the H. M. Suckling, the match 
going to four sets, bu-t Sherwell's low swift screw balls into the comers 
of the court, and his deadly volleying at the net proved sure winners. 
Foulkes, the former Canadian Champion, came down'to defend his Cup, 
and this was the best match of the series, several hundred people sur- 
rounding the championship court when tiie game startea. Foulkes 
had not had much practise and was off his game, though he put up a 
plucky fight to the end. Some of the rallies were beautiful, Sherwell 
playing at the net most of the time and Foulkes preferring the back of 
the court Sherwell won, 6-4, 6-2, 6-2. 

The Open Doubles were won by A. C. Dunlop and H. M. Suckling, 
this pair not losing a set during the tournament, although a 12-10 in the 
Finals was a close call. Veysey and Drew, both Englishmen, were the 
runners-up, and gave a beautiful exhibition of Doubles, their play at 
times being spectacular, but not steady enough. Miss Lois Moyes of 
Toronto was on hand to defend her Cup. Her play was far ahead of the 
local ladies pitted against her and she won the Cup for the third time. 
It now becomes her property. Miss Evelyn Clay beat Miss Hague 
and was the challenger for the Cup. A dark horse, E. E. Palmer, with 

171 



172 Canadian Lawn Tennis 

a big handicap, won the Handicap and also Novice Singles. The tour- 
nament was a success from everv point of view. The weather was per- 
fect and the courts in fine condition. 

Limch and afternoon tea were served on the grounds every day, the 
attendance was good, and the gallery most enthusiastic. Dinners and 
theatre parties for the visitors in which the ladies joined were the order 
every night, and everyone voted the tournament a big success. The 
Cups and prizes were given out by the president of the dub, M. A. D. 
Anderson at the close of the tournament on Wednesday.

INTERNATIONAL TOURNAMENT. 



The old and attractive annual International Lawn Tennis tournament 
took place as usual on the courts of the Queens Royal Hotel, Nia^ra- 
on-the Lake, Canada, on August 25 and following days and there is no 
better place to spend a week's outing for amusement or rest There 
was a good entry list of over forty in the Singles and sixteen teams in 
Doubles. Nat. NUes of Harvard University won the open after a hard 
match with Wallace Johnson of Philadelpnia who should really have 
been the victor, but the play was to often delayed owing to Niles being 
taken with a cramp in his leg which threw Jolmson off Ins game. Niles 
in his match in the Challenge Round against Irving Wright after being 
completely out-played by Wright in the two first sets got started and 
played some very lively tennis, winning the next three sets and with it 
the title of International Champion. 

The Doubles were won by Emerson and Wright; the Ladies' Singles 
bv Miss Wagner of West Side Club of New York. Thus ended a most 
pleasant week's tennis, particularly to the guests of the hotel and visitors 
from Toronto and Buffalo.

ONTARIO CHAMPIONSHIP. 



That tennis is rapidly obtaining a stronghold on the affections of 
the Canadian people was evidenced by the mrge entry list and enthu- 
siasm with which the Ontario Championships were run off at the courts 
of the Toronto Tennis Club in the first week of September, 1908. 

The weather throughout the week was ideal and play was continuous 
each morning from 10 o'clock until dusk. 

In addition to all the Toronto experts there was a large entry list from 
outside places. We had the pleasure of renewing our acquaintance 
with Jonnnie AUen, recently of New York, but now of Cobalt, 
whom all readers of the "Guide" will remember as the Doubles partner 
of H. H. Hadcett. Allen displayed all his old-time skill and ex- 
perienced httle difficulty in gettmg into the Finals of the Open Sindbes. 
Here he inet Robert BaiiQ of the Rusholme Tennis Club. The 
match between these two men brought out the best tennis of the year 
and Baird only succeeded in winning out after the hardest kind of a

struck. Allen took the first set quite easily and time and again was 
within a stroke of the second set, but Baird never lost his nerve and 
eventually wore his lighter opponent down, winning by the following 
score, 4-6, 14-12, 8-6,t6-4. This well-earned victory won for Baird 
the right to challenge the Ontario Champion, E. R. Faterson, the 
holder of the Cup. Paterson did not play with his old-time vigor and 
Baird won the match in straight sets by 6-4, 6-4, 7-5. 

The Finals in the Men's Championship Doubles resulted in a stub- 
bom contest between the old Toronto Club veterans, McMasber and 
Glassco, and Bums and Hall, the latter jMiir eventually winning by 
6-4, 7-5, 6-4. This victory entitled the winners to diallenge Messr. 
Baird and Witchall, holders of the Cup, who successfully defended 
their title, winning from the Toronto pair in three close sets, 6-4, 6-4, 7-5. 

Miss Ix)is Moyes, the Canadian Lady Champion, retained her title 
without difficulty, defeating Mrs. Belson of Welland, Ontario, in straight 
sets. 

George Wagner of Buffalo, L. Wick of Cleveland, and the 
redoubtable Harry Avery of Detroit, were guests of the Toronto 
Club during the tennis week. 



QUEBEC CHAMPIONSHIPS. 



The Province of Quebec Lawn Tennis Association held their annual 
tournament on the courts of the Mount Royal Lawn Tennis Club during 
the first two weeks in September, 1908. Owing to the fact that the big 
tournament the Canadian Championship, had been held here earlier 
in the summer, it was only natural that it should interfere to someVx- 
tent with this one. No outside clubs were represented, the tournament 
really being a city one, at the same time the local players showed keen 
interest and some fine matches marked the fmal games. J. Y. 
Sherwell again proved his superiority by successfully defending the 
Quebec Championship Cup. As this is nis third successful win, the 
large silver Cup now becomes his property. His opponent in the Chal 
lenge Round was A. C. Dimlop, who had a great fight in the Finals 
G. W. Grier. These two had met in several Finals before and each was 
determined to win. The match took the best part of three hours and 
the score will show how stubbornly it was fought out Dunlop finally 
won, 8;|6, 6-3, 5-7, 9-7. Sherwell beat Dunlop, 7-5, 6-3, 6-3. 

A. Grier, a young player with a big handicap, played very steadily 
and ^ won the Handicap easily. Mrs. McCullock, the holder of the 
I:rfidies' Championship was in England and could not defend her title, 
which Miss Hague won.

NOVA SCOTIA LAWN TENNIS ASSOCIATION. 



The eighth annual tournament of this association was held on August 
10 to 15 at Truro, Nova Scotia under the auspices of the Truro Lawn 
Tennis Club, who placed their beautiful grounds at the disposal of the 
association. There was a large number of clubs representea and more 
players took part than last year and the playing, taking it all through, 
was of higher order. A feature of the tournament was the playing for 
the Championship in the Men*s Doubles between W. Ritchie and 
F. T. Hands^nbody, Wanderers' Club versus A. C. Wiswell and 
E. Foster, South End Club, the latter players winning out. 

According to the rules the winners of last year's Championship could 
not take part in the tournament but were called upon to defend their 
title against the successful ones of this year 's tournament who were as 
follows:

popular umpire.

to get through nicely taking the whole week. -The Truro Club furnished 
entertainment of a varied sort consisting of a Garden Party at Senator 
Mackay's, a dance at the Agricultural Hall, also an excursion on special 
tram to Tolleigh Lake, and were unceasing in their efforts to give all a 
good time. Everyone present during the tournament enjoyed them- 
selves from a tennis standpoint and also socially. 

The St. Greorge's Lawn Tennis Club of Dartmouth, Nova Scotia, 
kindly invited the association to hold its tournament on their ground 
next year, 1909, which invitation was accepted. 

The association is also thinking if at all possible of getting the Canadian 
Championship meet held here next year, 1909, and in event of this we 
would be pleased to have some American players visit us. Grass and 
clay courts are used in this province, but the tendency is for day courts, 
and no doubt next year will see more of the clay court.

NEW BRUNSWICK TENNIS ASSOCIATION TOURNAMENT. 



The fifth annual tournament of the New Brunswick Tennis Associa- 
tion was held at Rothesay, in August The weather was favorable for 
tennis and the entry list the largest in the history of the Association. 
T. Malcolm McAvity of the St John Club won the Championship of 
New Brunswick for ihe second successive year.

Men's Doubles.

At the close of the tournament the Brunswick winners met the Nova 
Scotia representatives on the Rothesay courts for the Maritime Province 
Championships. T. Malcolm McAvity, the N. B. Champion, defeated 
his old rival C. R. Grant, the holder of the Maritime Province Cham- 
pionship last year, three sets to two. The match was played before a 
large number of spectatoi;^ and was pronoimced the best and most ex- 
citing tennis ever seen in this Province. After losing the first two sets, 
2-6, 3-6, Mr. McAvity won the remaining three, 10-8, 6-2, 6-2. The 
other events resulted as follows:

and T. M. McAvity (New Brunswck), 6-4, 11-9. 

A pleasing feature of both tournaments was the delightful manner in 
which the Rothesay Club entertained the visiting players. 



Canadian Lawn Tennis 185 

CHAMPIONSHIP OF THE MAINLAND OF BRITISH COLUMBIA. 



The annual open tournament of the Vancouver I^wn Tennis Club, 
for the Championships of the Mainland was held on the grounds of the 
above club in Vancouver, B. C, during the week commencing July 20, 
1908; B. P. Sdiwengers won the Championship in the Men*s Singles, 
defeating L. R. Freeman in the Finals in a four-set match. Schwengers' 
great staying quaUties proved too much for the Calif omian, and after 
two whirlwind sets (one to each) the British Columbia Champion took 
the next two, 6-2, 6-0. This was the third year in succession that 
Schwengers won this Championship, defeating all the best players on 
the Coast, and he fully demonstrated his supremacy on grass courts over 
all players in the North Pacific International Lawn Tennis Association. 

J. C. Tyler and L. R. Freeman had no difficulty in winning the Men *s 
Doubles. In the Finals they defeated C. Jordan and C. J. Hall in three 
straight sets. 

^Css Hotchkiss of won the Ladies' Singles handily, although Miss 
Beckett of Vancouver gavef her a good game in the Finals. 

Miss Hotchkiss and Miss Beckett won the Ladies' Doubles without 
much difficulty. 

Men's Singles. 

Preliminary Round: W. M. Griffin beat J. G. Fordham, 6-8, 6-3, 6-1 ; 
T. H. Bowden beat A. C. Eddy, 6-0, 2-6, 6-2, H. V. Ardagh beat E. W.

PENNSYLVANIA STATE TOURNAMENT. 



Merion Cricket Club, Haverford, Pa. 

Tennis continued during 1908 to hold the interest of a large part of 
the membership of the Merion Cricket Club, and despite me trying 
drought of the sununer, the turf was kept in good condition, and the 
tennis courts were better than those of other nearby dubs. 

Four important tennis toiunaments were held on the club's grounds 
during the summer and afforded pleasure alike to the players and spec- 
tators. 

The season opened with the annual Women 's Championship of Penn- 
sylvania and Eastern states which was held June 1 and following days 
and included the Championships in Women's Singles, Women's Doubles 
Mixed Doubles and also Consolation Matches in the various events. 

The tournament was won by Miss Eleanora Sears of the Longwood 
C. C, Boston, who also won the Championship for 1908 through the 
default of Miss Edith Rotch of the same club, who was unable to 
<lefend her Championship. 

The Championship in Doubles was won by Miss Eleanora Sears 
and Miss Marion Fenno, both of the Longwood C. C. 

llie Championship in Mixed Doubles were won by Miss Fenno and 
George Wrenn, who defeated Miss Sears and William J. Clothier, the 
1907 holders, in a hard-fought five-set match by 4-6, 6-1, 6-4, 11-9, 6-4. 

The Pennsylvania State Championship. 

The State of Pennsylvania Men's tournament was held June 8 and 
following davs and bemg open to all players had a large entry list, not 
less than thu*ty-four players taking part in the tournament, including 
j)layers from the Universities of Pennsylvania and Princeton, the Phila- 
delphia C. C, Germantown C. C, Belmont C. C Huntingdon Valley 
Country Club and other clubs. 

The tournament was won by Dr. E. B. Dewhurst, who defeated W. H, 
Trotter in a hard-fought contest. Dr. Dewhurst, also won the Penn- 
sylvania State Championship for 1908 by defeating J. R. Carpenter, Jr., 
of tJie Germantown C. C, 6-0, 6-0, 9-7.

The Merion Women's Tennis Team won the Interdub Tennis Cup 
for 1908, and Merion players reached the Finals or Semi-Finals in 
various events held durmg the season. 

The Club Championship in Men's Singles was won by Wallace F. 
Johnson and the Women's Club Championship was won by Miss Green. 

The All-Philadelphia Women's Tournament was held on Septem- 
ber 21 and following days. This tournament is held in turn ^ the 
various tennis clubs near Philadelphia, and the Merion Cricket Club's 
turn came this year. 

There was an excellent list of entries, and the Women 's Championship 
in Smgles was won by Miss Ilarlan. 

The Women's Championship in Doubles was won by Mrs. Gilbert 
and Mrs. Hibbs. 

The Intercollegiate tournament was held October 5 and followinq^ 
days at Merion Club courts, and representatives from eight colleges took 
pMt, N. W. Niles of Harvard winning the Championship in Singles 
after a closely-contested four-set match, and Tilden and Thaver 
of the University of Pennsylvania winning the Championship in Doubles, 
after playing eight sets, the first five sets having be^ played until dark- 
ness set in and the match was postponed, and upon being resumed was 
taken up from the start, the University of Pennsylvania players winning 
in three closely contested sets. 

The tennis committee of the club consists of Howard W. Lewis, chair-

Horace H. Sayres, Henry P. Bailey, H. Bartol Register, R. J. Crozier,

RHODE ISLAND STATE TOURNAMENT. 



The Rhode Island state tournament opened the week of September 5 
on the courts of the Agawam Hunt Club, "E&st Providence. J. D. E. 
Jones, for the third time, won the Championship, which made tlie 
handsome silver cup his personal property. The Doubles Championship 
was again won by the two old-time players, M. G. Chace and C. R. Bud- 
long, who showed their skill gained by past experience at the game. 

It has been decided that in the future the champions will have to play 
through the tournament. This is found to be the wish of a greater 
majority of the players.

CONNECTICUT STATE TOURNAMENT. 



The fourth Open State Championship of Connecticut was held on 
the courts of the Litchfield Club, Litchfield, Conn., July 14 and balance 
of the week. There was a good entry list and many interesting matches 
were played both in Singles and Doubles. An enthusiastic gallery 
was present during the week who were much interested in the different 
events that took place. 

The tournament was pronounced the most successful ever held in 
Litchfield. With dinners, dances and other entertainments the visiting 
players enjoyed a very pleasant week's outing.

MARYLAND STATE TOURNAMENT. 



The annual open tournament for the Maryland State Championship 
began on the courts of the Baltimore Country Club commencmg Sep- 
tember 9 and lasted into the following week. 

The entry list contained many strong out-of-town players which 
assured a good week's tennis. 

The playing of Robert LeRoy (fresh from his victory at Cincinnati) 
all through the tournament was of the best and made him the winner 
of the same and a right to challenge for the Championship Bowl held 
by Dr. Dewhurst, whom he defeated in three straight sets. The Doubles 
was won by Dr. Dewhurst and J. A. C. Colston after three hard-fought 
sets. 

A large gallery was present to witness the final matches of the tourna- 
ment.

CHAMPIONSHIP OF WESTERN PENNSYLVANIA 



The twentieth annual o^en tennis tournament for the Championship 
of Western Pennsvlvania in Singles was held on the grounds of the 
Pittsburgh Golf Club, Pittsburgh, Pa., on Monday, July 6 and following 
days. The tournament was the most successful and interesting that has 
ever taken place in this district, and attracted the best class of entries. 
There were 31 entries in the Singles, with such players as C. L. Childs, 
who held the title three times as Western Pennsylvania Champion; 
G. D. Edwards, former champion; E. Sunstein, formerly of Princeton: 



202 State Tournaments 

P. D. Siverd, former Interscholastic Champion; C. M. Fincke, the old 
Yale baseball and football player; L. D. Waddell, the Yale baseball 
player; E. M. Byers, of national goW fame, and T. W. Stephens, a former 
Western Pennsylvania Champion. 

In the Final Round Childs defeated Sunstein, 7-5, 4-6, 8-6, 6-4, after 
a very hard match in which both players fought for the position at the 
net. Both players were smashing and volleving beautifully and the 
match brou^t out the prettiest tennis that has been played in Pittsburgh 
for some time. 

In the Challenge match for the J. C. Grogan Cup, C. L. Childs 
(challenger) defeated W. L. Myers (defender), 9-7, 6-4, 6-3. Childs 
was very steady throughout and Myers* form seemed to indicate a lack 
of hard practice, as he did not seem to be able to control his serve or 
ground stroke. 

The audience was the largest that has ever attended a tennis tourna- 
ment in Pittsburgh and was very appreciative of the many fine plays 
that were made. 

C. L. Childs now holds one leg on the new Challenge Cup which was 
won in 1907 by W. L. Myers and in 1906 by T. W. Stephens, the previous 
Cup having been won three times by C. L. Childs, and consequently 
became his personal property. 

The Scores:

NORTHWESTERN PENNSYLVANIA TOURNAMENT. 



The third tournament of Northwestern Pennsylvania Lawn Tennis 
Association was held at Warren, Pa., August 3 to 7, under the 
auspices of the Warren Tennis Club. Sixteen clubs comprise the 
membership of this association, being from the following places: Erie, 
Franklin, Oil City, Dubois, St. Mary's Smethport, Lockiiaven, Belle- 
fonte, Johnsonburg, Warren, Tidioute, Kane, Ridgway, Bradford and 
Eldred. Thirty-seven entries were received in Singles and nineteen 
in Doubles. 

Marked improvement in almost every team entered was shown over 
the play of previous years. It was also decided that hereafter there 
should be no Challenge Round, but that all contestants should play 
through the tournament on the same basis. There was a keen interest 
taken in the tournament both by players and spectators, which gives 
every promise of the coming fourth annual tournament which is to take 
place at Franklin, Pa., being more successful than former tournaments. 

Championship Singles. 

Prelimmary Round: M. D. Crary (Warren) beat Forman (Kahkwa 
Club, Erie), by default; Gephart (Bellefonte) beat Dietrick (Johnson- 
burg), 2-6, 6-2, 6-2; Lyle (Smethport) beat Spencer (Erie), 6-4, 6-0; 
Cleveland (Kahkwa Club) beat Hunter (St. Mary's), 6-2, 6-1; Lane 
(Bellefonte) beat Mackey (Franklin), 6-8, 7-5, 6-2.

VIRGINIA STATE TOURNAMENT. 



The eighth annual lawn tennis tournament for the Championship of 
Virginia was held under the auspices of the Country Club of Norfolk, 
Va., during the week of June 15, 1908. 

The weather conditions were excellent, all that could have been 
wished for, and although the number of out-of-town entries was not 
large, great interest was created by the plajdng of Franklin Greoghegan 
of Washington, D. C, who won all his matches against the best Norfolk 
players and the Championship Round from Hugh G. Whitehead, who 
already had two legs on the trophy. 

Messrs. Wash, Reed and Hugh G. Whitehead won for the second 
time the Championship in Doubles without much opposition, and Miss 
M. C. Evans of Richmond won the Ladies' Championship in an in- 
teresting match against Mrs. Wallington Hardy of Norfolk. 

Li 1^9 the Country Club is anxious to have more players from other 
cities, and will probably hold the Virginia tournament later in June in 
order to enable more college men to be present. 

The tennis season in Norfolk was the most successful and enthusiastic 
in its history and the local players are very proud that Norfolk's rep- 
resentatives at Atlanta, Ga., J. H. Winston, and Hugh G. Whitehead 
were winners of the Championship of the South, and therefore com- 
peted at Bay Ridge against the East and West. 

Although their showing in New York was a disappo'ntment to their 
friends, me experience will prove very beneficial to Southern players, 
and will result m later years m closer competitions in the Double Cham- 
pionship among the East, West and South. 

Norfolk's new Country Club, situated on a be^tiful body of land in 
full view of the river, will be completed this winter, and will make a 
most attractive spot for the Virginia tournaments in the future.

WEST VIRGINIA STATE CHAMPIONSHIP. 



The ideal weather conditions, the large and fashionable gatherings 
of enthusiastic spectators, the splendid courts and the high class tennis 
exhibited, all contributed to the unqualified success of the third annual 
Invitation tennis tournament, for the Championship of West Virginia, 
held at the Parkersburg Country Club on August 24, 25 and 26, 1908. 
Representative players from all parts of the state were present to com- 
pete for the handsome Challenge Cup, and no effort was spared by the 
management to make the stay of the visitors most pleasant and a^eeable. 
Numerous informal affairs and the big club dance given in honor of 
the visiting players on the last day, were the social features of the week. 

Deserving of special mention was the excellent form displayed by 
E. I. Hanlon, of Sistersville, W. Va., who played through to the Final 
Round, and by the default of Everett Drennen, earned the right to 
challenge the holder of the three-year Cup, Ward White. 

Hanlon, however, seemed to be unable to get going in the Challenge 
match, which was quite a disappointment, on account of the splendid 
showing he had made during nis previous play, he was looked upon as 
having a fine chance to win, however, White won in three straight sets, 
thus giving the victor his second leg on the Challenge Cup. 

Individual Cups were presented to E. I. Hanlon, winner in Singles, 
and to White and Drennen, winners in Doubles. 

Handsome watch fobs were presented to the runner-up in Singles, 
Everett Drennen, and to the runners-up in Doubles, Hubbard and 
Fleming. 

Beautiful jBasks were also presented to R. A. Johnson, winner in 
Singles, and to Bowie and Park, winners in Doubles in the Consolation 
events.

TRI-STATE TOURNAMENT. 



Ohio, Indiana, Kentucky. 



The tenth annual open tournament, held under the auspices of the 
Ohio Ijawn Tennis Association for the Tri-State Championship on the 
courts of tlie Cincinnati Club, was a blue ribbon tennis feature. Those 
of us who were fortunate enough to be present have ample cause for 
congratulation, and those of you who were not there should certainly 
include Cincinnati in your 1909 lawn tennis itinerary. Under the 
leadership of P. B. Stanberry the tournament committee gave a 
most refreshing demonstration of the proper way to conduct a tourna- 
ment. And those followers of the game who have had practical ex- 
I)erience in tournament manajrement will i^eadily appreciate the magni- 
tude of tlie task that confronts a committee which would successfully 
pilot a bi¬´? tournament embracing five separate events and an entry list 
of one hundred and sixty individual entries. In the opinion of the writer 
the Tri-State tournament is surpassed by no tennis feature on the sched- 
ule. We are accustomed to see large galleries and mammoth entry 
lists at Newport and Ixjngwood, and well remember and cherish dearly 
the courtesy which all receive at Southampton and Orange. But here 
in this Middle W'est feature we find a gallery which for numbers, en- 
thusiasm and "tennis sense" is certainly not surpassed at any tourna- 
ment in the I nited States. For the most part the grand stana at New- 
port is occupied by those who are present, not l)ecause of their tennis 
enthusiasm, but simply because it is the proper thing to do at that par- 
ti(Hilar season of the year, whereas at the Tri-State we see a gallery each 
member of which is a tennis enthusiast. And the feminine portion of 
that Cincinnati gallery certainly losses nothing by comparison with 
the Newport elite. And in addition to this most seemly gallery the visit- 
ing i)layers are at all times surrounded by the Southampton-Orange 
type of courtesy. The pleasure of the visiting players was also gready 
enhanced through the eii'orts of the chairman of the entertainment com- 

^ttee, J. P. Kidney, who is by far the most artistic dispenser of ‚Ä¢ 
' refreshmant that Cincinnati can boast. No matter how many 



State Tournaments 211 

miles separate you from Cincinnati, the last of August should see you 
on your way to the Tri-State if you wish to experience one of the richest 
of lawn tennis experiences. 

The tournament of 1908 was not up to the usual standard in the way 
of entries. For one reason or another such sterling players as Little 
Palmer, Touchard, Mollenhauer and Dewhurst were compelled, at the 
last moment, to wire their inability to be present. The Eastern contin- 
gent, which ordinarily is extremely stronsr at this tournament, was thus 
reduced to Robert LeRoy, the holder, Chauncey Seaver, S. W. Merri- 
hew and P. B. Hawk. The "star" entry of the tournament was, of 
course, the redoubtable "Nat" Emerson. This sterling player, fresh 
from his Newport successes where he achieved the most notable vic- 
tories of any Western player since the palmy days of Kreierh Collins, 
and bearing the weight of his Western and Northwestern Champion- 
ships upon his shoulders, was to demonstrate his prowess upon his 
native heath. He is the idol of all Cincinnati and a well chosen idol 
is he. Exponents of the game may well give full meed of praise to 
players possessing the true sportsmanship of Emerson. 

The contests throughout the tournament went according to "past 
performances" so far as the main entries were concerned. Emerson 
defeated in succession Hawk and Seaver, both in five-set matches, 
the latter being a "hair raising" exhibition in which Seaver played 
excellent tennis, but Emerson was finally victorious. However, the 
really "big" event of a Championship tournament is, of course, the 
Challenge Match. In this case it brought Robert LeRoy and the idol- 
ized "Nat" into the roped arena. The class of the contestants augured 
well for a spectacular, long drawn out and hard-fought combat. It was 
not to be, however, for it was at once apparent, that Emerson was not 
in the form which had won him his season's laurels. We do not mean 
by this to detract one whit from the honor due to LeRoy. This most 
excellent player would certainly have won even with Emerson at his 
best for the classy tennis shown by the brilliant New Yorker was seldom 
excelled on any court during the season. Emerson was not in the least 
disgraced by his defeat, for none of the "topnotchers" excepting Cham- 
pion Lamed and Beals Wright (on his Newport or Australian form) 
could have sent Robert LeRoy to defeat the afternoon of his signal 
triumph over "Nat" Emerson. 

Miss Mariorie Dodd and Miss Martha Kinsey, who contested in the 
Final Round of Ladies' Singles, would certainly be able to set a nice 
fast pace for some of the Eastern "cracks" at St. Martins. Cincin- 
nati would be well represented if these young women were to try for 
Championship honors. 

In tne Men's Doubles Emerson and Hunt were at no time extended. 
The Mixed Doubles were captured by Miss McLaug:hlin and Dr. 
Karl Little who succeeded in handily winning from Miss Dodd and 



212 State Tournaments 

Dr. Hawk, and also came out victorious over Miss Kruse and Mr. 
Seaver after a rathier strenuous session.

ILLINOIS STATE TOURNAMENT. 



The first of the open tournaments was the one for the Illinois State 
Championship ‚Äî the seventh annual ‚Äî which was held, as usual, on the 
courts of the Aztec Tennis Club of Chicago on July 11 and following 
days. The entry list in the Men's Singles was unusually large, the 
Men 's Doubles drew well also, but lack of women entrants necessitated 
the cancelling of the Doubles and Mixed Doubles events for women. 
The play was unusually keen this year and many matches of great in- 
terest were contested in the early rounds.

WISCONSIN STATE TOURNAMENT. 



The annual Wisconsin State Championship took- place August 10 
on the courts of the Town Club, Milwaukee, Wis., and proved one of 
the most successful tennis tournaments ever held by the club. 

The ent¬ªy list was a large one made up mostly of strong players, 
among them were several out of town players. The tennis played dur- 
ing the week was of the best and the winner of the tournament, R. J. 
Crozier, last year's Champion, had to work hard to retain his title 
which he did after playing several well-contested matches. 

Dindng the last days of the tournament there was a large crowd pres- 
ent watching the different matches that took place.

State Tournaments 
MONTANA STATE TENNIS TOURNAMENT. 






The class of play and attendance at the fourteenth annual Montana 
state tournament held at Helena, Montana, August 17 to 22, was most 
gratifying. A week of beautiful weather, augmented greatly by the 
hospitality, attendance and applause of the fair devotees of the game 
early in the tournament, put every visitor at ease, and as a result each 
[)ut forth his best efforts. The matches throughout were, with but 
few exceptions, hard fought, and in many instances, closer than the 
final scores would indicate. 

The matches between Mosher of Butte, and Agar of Great Falls, 
Johnson of Helena and Mattison of Great Falls, Karsted of Butte and 
Burton of Butte, were perhaps the most noteworthy of the tournament, 
the class of tennis being of high order in each and the results uncertain 
until the last points had been played. 

Burton of Butte, through brilliant and consistent work during the 
entire tournament, without question earned the right to challenge State 
Champion D. D. McLaughlin of Butte for supreme honors. This 
long, nard-fought five-set match was fraught with added interest by 
reason of the fact that its outcome was to decide, in case of a third victory 
by Champion McLaughlin, the ownership of the beautiful silver trophy, 
which for a decade and more has been the unattainable goal of every 
tennis player in the state. 

The weather was perfect and the audience large, enthusiastic and im- 
partial, and the fact that Burton lost, and McLEiughlin, the grand old 
man of the tennis game in this state, won, is but another illustration of 
the old axiom that every rule has its exceptions. Youth did not conae 
out victorious; McLau^jnlin, with his half century and more of years, 
perfect physical condition and untiring energy and unfailing accuracy 
and precision, emerged from the contest a winner, and apparently as 
fresh as at its commepcement 

The business meeting resulted, after a spirited controversy, in the 
selection of an eastern aty for the next meeting place of the tournament 
in 1909, the Livingston club, sponsors of the Hunters' Hot Springs' 
courts, convincing the meeting that the interests of tennis in Mx>ntana 
would be best subserved by such a choice. The state officers elected 
were the following: President, A. R. Mosher, Butte; vice-president, 
J. D. Miles, Livingston; secretary-treasurer, George M. Parker, Living- 
ston. 

The Scores: 

Men's Singles. 

Preliminary Round: Smith (Great Falls) beat Davenport ^Billings); 
Burton (Butte) beat Hunter (Anaconda), by default; Parker (Livingston) 
beat Det'orest (Great Falls); Agar (Great Falls) beat J. Railey (Ana-

OKLAHOMA STATE TOURNAMENT. 



The first tennis tournament for the Championship of Oklahoma 
State took place at Oklahoma City the week of September 1 on the courts 
of the Putnam Park Club. The winner of the tournament, J. T. Bailey, 
who has made a good showing in many of the western events during 
the summer won the tournament by steady play and the handling of 
his well balanced Ward & Wright racket with good effect. 

This was the first state tournament held in Oklahoma and was pro- 
nounced quite a success. The Championship of the coming season is 
looked forward to with interest.

Country Club. The week's tennis proved a most pleasant one to 
players and spectators alike.

went by default to MacQuiston.

OREGON STATE TOURNAMENT. 



Portland felt proud. Indeed Portland felt very proud, for in the 
summer time came journeying from all directions a delegation of tennis 
players that would certainly grace the courts of any clime or country. 

From the Sunny South came Lou Freeman, who renewed his old- 



^34 State Tournaments 

time acquaintance and once more looked upon the Cups he used to take 
away so handily. 

From Berkeley came that delightful and wonderful player, Miss Hazel 
Hotchkiss, and from Spokane came that stalwart, bright and shining 
light in Pacific Northwest tennis, Wallace A. McBumey, with his left- 
handed partner, Joseph Tyler. 

Many lesser lights appeared on the scene and to make a perfect tourna- 
ment Portland enjoyed the privilege of entertaining^ the guests on the 
grounds of the Irvington Tennis Club. 

Everyone in Irvington kept open house and the week was full of 
enjoyment. An informal party on Monday night at the club house 
started the social activities and with several luncheons, an evening at 
"The Oaks," an auto touring evening and the Tennis Ball on Friday 
night, the week was quite complete. 

Of tennis there can much be said and since Portland was the only 
tournament in which Wallace A. McBumey played, aside from his 
home town, it probably takes first place in the way of prominence in 
the Northwest circuit for 1908, smce McBumey was ranked first 
by the ranking committee of the North Pacific International Lawn 
lennjs Association and were it possible, he doubtless would secure a 
rank up next to the top for the Pacific Coast. 

What a pity he could not go to Del Monte this sununer. 

The wins were clean cut and without any question of doubt. 

Miss Hotchkiss set at rest any chance for outsiders in the Ladies' 
Singles, Ladies' Doubles and Mixed Doubles. 

Unfortunately Portland lost an opportunity in presenting a firet-class 
team of Men's Doubles, and this event was not of any special interest 
and went quite easily to McBumey and Tyler. 

The Gentlemen's Singles was a different proposition, and to follow 
the winner through his maze of wins and difficult situations takes mental 
activities worthy of a Napoleon. Several matches stand out as mile 
posts in his progress. Goss was his first man of any prominence, but 
he did not make much more than a reasonably decent fight. "Mac" 
had to to keep his wits about him but he was not pressed. 

Jordan was the next trader who negotiated for the place. Here was 
some of the prettiest tennis ever played. AVhat a wonder Jordan would 
make were ne able to control himself and had a little better physique. 

Lou Freeman should not have taken part in the Singles competition" 
for he was imable to keep up the pace set by men in good trim and he 
was forced to default to McBumey after showing good form, although 
he did not show that he ever possessed the wonderful play of the latter. 

The Challenge Roimd was somewhat of a foregone conclusion. Tyler 
and McBumey had met many times before, and while Mac occasionally 
has an off day he had the thmg pretty well in hand when he faced Joe 
for the Oregon State Championship. Tyler is a fine player with no 
end of endurance and a wonderful propensity for doing the impossible. 



State Toubnaments 235 

He can stand on his head and play a half volley on his back hand at the 
same time, but this won't do against the tremendous game of Wallace 
A. McBurney. 

McBumey is the same kind of a man that could get a lot of men over 
the Delaware, he would have captured Port Arthur if given the chance, 
and lie would be just the fellow to tackle Jim Jeffries if he were so in- 
clined and big enough. 

He has the i)hysique, the head, the tremendous capacity for work and 
the determination to do a good, clean job on a tennis court. 

We in Portland take our hats off to our Champion, Wallace A. Mc- 
Bumey.

CENTRAL IOWA STATE TOURNAMENT ASSOCIATION. 



The third annual tournament of the above association was held on the 
the courts of the Toledo (Iowa) Lawn Tennis Club at Toledo, Iowa, July 
28-31, 1908, and was one of the best ever held in the state. 

H. L. Beyer, Orinnell, Iowa, the 1907 Champion in Singles, again 
proved his prowess by defeating in a fierce, fast and uncertain contest 
C. H. Gooayear of Cedar Rapids, Iowa. 

In the Doubles contest the 1907 Champions, Kauffman and Trumbull, 
Conrad, Iowa, also retained their laurels in the 1908 meet, worsting 
their competitors, Stiger and St Clair (Toledo), in a four-set series. 

The annual meets of this association are of great interest to the lovers 
of good tennis in this part of the Middle West, and much interest Ls al- 
ready being shown in the coming 1909 meet which will be held at Toledo, 
Iowa, July 6-9, 1909, and will be open to all comers. 

The Scores: 

Men's Singles.

year, 0-6, 6-1, 7-5, 1-6, 6-4.

MASSACHUSETTS STATE DOUBLES TOURNAMENT. 



The Massachusetts State Doubles Championship took place on the 
fine clay courts of the Brae-Bum Country Club, West Newton, 
Mass., May 30 to June 6. Outside of the Longwood tournament this 
is one of the most important events in tennis in or about Boston, as it 
brings together our best players who are located in this part of the country, 
which is sure to cause many exciting matches to be played and always 
brings out a large and interesting gallery. 

Beals Wright and SuUoway won the tournament by superior play, 
which entitled them to play Johnson Brothers (Charlie Johnson being m 
poor health for a week before the tournament naturally prevented them 
from playing their best game), holders of the Cup. 

Wnght was at his best and with Sulloway playing his usual steady 
game they proved the victors and the honor of the title of being the 
Massachusetts Doubles Champions.

TOURNAMENTS OF 1908. 



The Metropolitan Open tournament commenced June 16 with the 
usual large entry list on the day courts of the West Side Tennis Club, 
New York City. Several of the best players who took part in the tourna- 
ment last year did not play. Alexander, who won the Cup outright 
last vear did not take part in Singles. This left it to the younger players 
of wnom Ross Buchard came through the tournament a winner, defeat- 
ing G. F. Touchard by the score of 6-4, 9-7, 5-7, 6-1. The Doubles 
were won by Hackett and Alexander defeating Imng Wright and Tor- 
rence in straight sets, 6-2, 6-2, 6-2. ‚Ä¢ 



The Kings County Tennis Club Open tournament began on the 
club's courts May 25 with a large entry list. The tournament was 
won by Fred Alexander, which entitled him to play Irving Wright, 
holder, whom he defeated rather easily by the score of 6-4, 6-2, 6-1. 

The Doubles were won by Hackett and Alexander defeating Ned 
Lamed and Watson, 6-2, 6-2, 6-2. 



The New York Tennis Club Open tournament began June 1 with a 
large entry list of the leading players in and about New York City, 
which caused many exciting games to be played. The tournament was 
won by T. R. Pell, who challenged and defeated Harold Hackett, holder 
of the Felijie (>up, in a well-played five-set match by the score of 5-7, 
6-4, 6-2, 3-6, 6-2. 

llie Doubles were won by Hackett and Alexander defeating Pell and 
Watson, 6-2, 6-2, 6-2. 



The tournament of the Englewood Field Club took place on their 
grounds at Englewood, N. J., July 11 and following days and was a 
great success in every way both in a good size entry list and large at- 
tendance throughout the week. The Singles were won by Palmer 
defeating Tallant, 6-3, 0-6, 6-3, 6-2. 

In Doubles Irving Wright and Torrence defeated Bull and Martin, 
5-7, 6-2, 6-2, 6-1.

NATIONAL INDOOR CHAMPIONSHIPS. 



HELD February 20, 22, 23, 24, 25, 2(5, 27. 
The year nineteen hundred and nine marks the tenth consecutive 
year, that the National Indoor Championships have been successuUy 
terminated at the Seventh Regiment Armory, New York City, and the 
championships which the Seventh Re<;iment inausfurated in February, 
1900, have each year increased in popularity, until 1909 with an entry 
list of sixty-one in Singles and twenty-seven in Doubles, broke all former 
records for an indoor lawn tennis tournament of any kind, and the name 
af Cragin, which family of four brothers originated the idea of the 
"National Indoor Championships" in 1900, as previous to that time 
the only Indoor Championships were "Handicap'* events and not 
sanctioned by the U. S. N. L. 1. A., were as much in 'evidence as ever 
and their interest and enthusiasm help to make every year a success. 

Theodore Roosevelt Pell, Champion of 1907, won the Championship 
without the loss of a set, tlie first time this has been accomplished since 
the tournament started in 1900, and Wylie C. Grant, four times Cham- 
pion, for the first time in eight years missed the Final Round by beingf 
defeated by Pell in the Fourth round, 16-14, 9-7, in a gruelling match 
after Grant in the first set had led with the vantage game and 40-15 
These two sets with Grant which were" close all the way, and the first set 
against W. B. Cragin, Jr. in the Semi-Finals, when Cragin led Pell in 
the first set, 4-0, and 40-15, were the nearest that Pell came to losing a 
set In the Finals against G. Carleton Shafer, although Pell did not 
show at his best, Shafer made an imexpectedly fine showing leading 3-2 
in the first and third sets, being defeated 6-4, 6-3, 6-4. 

The Doubles were somewhat of a repetition of the Singles inasmuch 
as nearly all the favorites were drawn in the upper half. H. H. Hackett, 
last year's Champion in Doubles, with F. B. Alexander played witli 
Raymond D. Little, but after two rounds were defeated by T. R. Pell 
and W. C. Grant, 7-5, 7-5, who went through the tournament without 
the loss of a set, narrowly escaping in the last set of the Final Round 
when after defeating \V. B. Cragin, Jr., and M. S. Charlock the first 
two sets, 6-3, 6-2, Cragin and Charlock led 5-4, 40-15. 

C. F. Touchard won tlie Consolation in a five-set match.

LAWN TENNIS NOTES. 



In referring to their visit to Australia, the American team s^ak 
most highly of the way they were favored by the tennis committee 
who did evervthing to make their visit a pleasant one, while the umpire 
and linesmen s decisions were most satisfactory. 

It is sure to add a new interest to the game in the West by having the 
preliminaries in Doubles between teams from the East, South, West 
and Pacific Coast played on the grass courts of the Onwentsia Grolf 
Club, Lake Forest, which are located 30 miles out of Chicago, III. 
(See fixtures for date) the winner of which will be entitled to play 
the present Champions at Newport. 

Uj)on the visit of the Eastern players to California last season to take 
part in the Pacific States Championship, the home players showed a 
keen interest in watching and noticing how diflterent the style of play 
of the visitors was from their own, winning their strokes by placing 
the ball, while California players were more for winning their point by 
killing the ball or a severe passing stroke across court. What effect 
it wiU have on their play in going firom asphalt to ^ss courts is 
hard to tell, though it bothered me Eastern men but little m making the 
change. 

Beals Wright, upon his return home from his trip to Australia where 
he went as one of the team to play in the Davis Cup Matches, was given 
a dinner by his club, the Longwood Cricket Club, at which he gave 
a very interesting account of the hard-fought matches he and his partner 
Alexander had with the Australians, Brookes and Wilding. 

Mrs. Sterry, who won the English Championship last year, has 
won it four times before. She plays a steady all-round gams but 
not so brilliant or severe a stroke as tiie ex-Champion, Mrs. Chambers. 

A. W. Grore, winnCT of the English Championship, has also won 
it three times. He is over forty years of age; his play is princi- 
pMdly from the back of the court, his strokes are accurate witli 
plenty of speed. He won the Indoor Championship the past winter. 

Players and those interested in the game throughout the country, 
should subscribe and add their support to "American Lawn Tennis," 
which has during the past two years proven itself worthy of their sup- 
port as the accounts of tournaments and items of interest with pictures 
of players and different club courts makes it a most attractive and in- 
teresting paper. Address S. W. Merrihew, Terminal Building, 41st St., 
and Park Avenue, New York City. 

249 



250 Lawn Tennis Notes 

After many years trying the English Lawn Tennis Association started 
last year a school tournament (the same idea as the Interscholastic 
tournament in this country) whidi was most successful and from whidi 
England hopes to get her future Lawn Tennis Champions. 

R. D. Wrenn, Beals Wright and Karl Behr were Interscholastic 
National Champions and have played on International teams. 

Tlie following was taken from an Australian paper: 

"Although defeated, it will doubtless be gratifying to our esteemed 
visitors and their Association to know that this visit, which brought 
about the first International Lawn Tennis Contest in Australia, has* 
given a tremendous stimulus, and has been of inestimable value to our 
favorite game in this quarter of the globe. 

"This report would not be complete without referring to the particu- 
larly favorable impression universally created by our visitors both off 
and on the courts. They, after being thoroughly tested, signally proved 
themselves to be true sportsmen and worthy representatives of their 
great nation. 

"Whilst justly lauding our opponents, we must not forget our own rep- 
resentatives. The heartiest thanks of our Association are due to 
N. E. Brooks and A. F. Wilding for so successfully and ably defending 
the coveted ('up. Both put forth their best efforts, and joined with 
our visitors in providing the finest display of lawn tennis ever seen in 
Australia or possibly elsewhere, and the most exciting and strenuous 
contest in the history of the International Matches." 

Read carefully the revised Foot Fault Rule on page 12. 

It is suggested that the most practical way to jud'^e a foot fault is for 
the umpire to watch only the server's feet, and to determine when the 
service is delivered, by the sound of the impact of racket and ball. 

The foot fault umpire should be in line with the base line. 

The following instructions were given to the linesmen at the Davis 
Cup IVIatclies played at I^ongwood last fall: 

The linesmen's position is a responsible one, therefore, his attention 
should be fixed on his duty and he should neither converse nor should 
his decisions be influenced by the remarks of others. 

Under no circumstances is a linesman to leave his post while the match 
is being played. 

The only words a linesman should use are "out" or "fault" and 
these should be called out loudly, distinctly and promptly enough so 
that both players and the umpire may understand; if, however, a player 
asks for a decision (how is it?) while the ball is in play, and the ball 
is good, the linesman should say "play" in a quick clear and loud voice. 
(Ine word "play" is preferable to "good.") 



Lawn Tennis Notes 251 

Never render a decision before the ball strikes the ground, but if it 
strikes "out'* as soon thereafter as possible. 

When a ball land^ obviously out no decision need be called, but all 
balls landing within 3 feet outside of the line taken by the linesman 
should be called "out" as the players are frequently not in the right 
position to see. 

A linesman is placed in a position to decide as best he can on the line 
in question and nis decision is final whether right or wrong. 

Fred H. Hovey, the winner of the National Championship in 1895, 
has played but little tennis since that time, and is now an expert at 
Squash Racquets, being one of the team who won the New England 
Championship. 

The Coronado Country Club tournament, held annually at Coronado, 
Cal., February 17 to 22, is the one in which the prominent tennis players 
of the Pacific Coast open their season. The different events this season 
were won as follows: Singles, McLaughlin; Doubles, Bundy and Sinsa- 
baugh; Ladies' Singles, Miss May Sutton; Mixed Doubles, Miss Sutton 
and Mr. Sinsabaugh.

In tournament matches the linesmen are carefully selected and usually 
players of experience who are impartial. Now if a wrong decision is 
made should a player who is benefitted "throw** the next point by de- 
liberately knocking the ball out or into the net ? Of course the average 
onlooker applauds, but this is entirely wrong. 

The players go on the court with the understanding to abide by the 
linesmen's and umpires* decisions and when they do not, it only cheapens 
them, makes them more timid in calling and harder for the committee 
to get men to serve as linesmen. It also puts the opposing player in a 
peculiar position. Such a thing should not be encouraged as decisions 
usually even up in a match. 

This point occurred several times last year and cannot be termc<l 
sportsmanlike. 



252 Lawn Tennis Notes 

Isaac Chambers, the experienced groundsman of the Longwood 
Club of Boston, gives the few following hints about grass courts: 

There is no hard and fast rule or method for taking care of grass courts. 
Work which would be all right one day might be all wrong the next. 
For example: At times during the season cutting the grass once a week 
is suflficient, while at another time two or three cuttings are required, and 
during tournaments they must be cut every day; but for club use twice 
a week is a fair average. It is possible, during hot weather, to cut grass 
too short. 

At Longwood, for four or five weeks before a big tournament we be- 
gin to pay a little extra attention to the lawn by way of watering a great 
deal, moving courts oftener. For example, some courts are not allowed 
to stay in one place more than one week, and others not more than three 
or four days. This prevents bare spots resulting from wear which would 
otherwise appear, no matter how small. 

For club use at Longwood any number of courts up to twenty are 
needed, according to the time of season. These are rolled and marked 
every day, a twelve hundred pound roller being used, with brush in 
front, two or three times per week, or if conditions are favorable a three 
ton roller is put on onpe or twice a week. This roller is drawn by one 
horse and cannot be used when the grass is wet, or when the courts are 
too heavy without danger to horse and damage to lawn. For a week 
before and during a tournament, this roller is put on every day, and it is 
surprising how much rolling a lawn will stand before kimng the grass. 
The courts should always be rolled and cut crosswise. 

In June, for the Massachusetts State Championship twenty courts 
are used, at Longwood. Fifteen are placed side by side, twelve feet 
apart. For the Longwood open tournament in July twelve courts are 
used, more room being allowed players, twenty feet at the side and twenty- 
five to thirty at the end of the courts. For play in international matches, 
twenty-five feet at the side and thirty at the end are allowed. 



History tells us that lawn tennis was first played with a cork ball, 
which was originally struck with the hand, much as in our present game 
of Hand-Ball, a bank of earth or mud about two feet high serving the 
same pu^ose as our modem net. As the game grew in severity a glove 
was used to cover the hand, as is often done today in Hand-Ball; then 
some pioneer introduced strings across the palm of the hand from thumb 
to fingers, and with these the ball was given greater impetus with less 
impact. It was a short jump from that to a frame-work for the strings, 
which was held first in the hand, and a handle for the frame soon fol- 
lowed, forming the crude model from which our familiar rackets used 
in so many ball games were all evolved. 



How to Make a Lawn Tennis Court 



In making a Lawn Tennis Court there should be, if 
possible, a dear margin of at least 12 feet on each side 
and 21 feet at each end of the court, or, between adjacent 
courts, 18 feet on each side; but should the courts be placed 
end to end, or end to side, there should be spaces of 42 
feet or 33 feet respectively, and a back-net at least 8 feet 
high between. 

The cheapest and most easy court to make is a 

Clay Court 

It is done by removing the sward from the lawn, taking 
care that the same is cut deep enough not to allow any of 
the grass roots to remain; there should be a space dug out 
118 feet by 56 and about 6 or ten inches deep, fill in with 
yellow day and cover with ^-inch of carefully sifted bind- 
mg gravel, taking out aU small stones to the size of a pea. 
wet well and roU into the foundation of clay, if the court 
continues to be soft or sticky spread more binding gravel 
over it, and continue to roll until the dav and eravd com- 
mences to bind and becomes firm. 1 he maker of the 
court will have to use his own best judgment about this, 
as it's hard to dedde the exact quantities of day and lind- 
ing gravd that should be used to cause the court to have 
the proper firmness. 

In making the court there should be a gradual slope 
from the base lines to the centre, running across the court 
between the net posts should be built a blind drain, this is 
done to take off tne water after a heavy rain. 

A stone foundation is bad for a court as it becomes hard 
and dry and severe on a player's feet and legs to play on. 

Grass Courts 

These can be made in three ways: namel^r. first, by 
mproving an existing stretch of turf until it is level 
and smooth enough, second by leveh'ng the ground.

254 How TO Make a Lawn Tennis Court 

preparing the soil, ami sowing grass seeds ; and third, 
by laying fresh turf. Of these the former is of course 
the simplest ; but as the requisite piece of gras*! land 
is rarely available, particulars for preparing it are 
seldom required. In improving such a piece of turf 
care should be taken to remove all weeds or coarse 
grasses, and io freely use the spirit-level. To raise 
hollow places the turf should be cut out, lifted, and 
fine soil placed underneath until the proper height 
has been obtained, when the turf should be replaced 
and well beaten down with the fiat side of a spade. 
On the other hand, small mounds should be removed 
by taking the soil away from under the grass, some 
fine mould being placed, underneath tne turf before 
it is replaced. To prepare a court for sowing seeds 
the same iiistructions may be followed as for making 
a turf- laid one, except that the seed will be sown 
instead of the turfs laid. Take care that the seed is 
-good by purchasing from a first-class seedsman, and, 
wnen ordering, it will be well to state what sort of 
soil is to be sown. By far the larger number of 
courts ^re made by laying fresh turfs. 

When preparing the ground for a court, it is de- 
sired to level a portion measuring 100 x 50 feet, 
which allows an ample margin ; but for the playing 
portion itself only 78 x b6 feet is necessary. If 
economy must be strictly considered only the latter 
need be carefully prepared, but it is better to have 
the whole done if possible. 



HOW TO MARK OUT A COURT 




Ag a doable court practicailj' includes every line to 
be found in a single coiut, it is best to take first the 
measure for the latter. Having determined the posi* 
tion of your net, plant in the pround, in the line 
chosen, two pegs, 27 feet apart (a* the points A and 
B in the diagram). Then take two measures and 
attach their respective ends to the pegs A and B. On 
the first, which wiD measure the diagonal of the court, 
take a length of 47 ft. 5 in. ; on the other 39 ft. ; poO 
both taut in such directions that at these distances 
they meet in a point C. This will give one comer of

lEe Wright (& Ditson Rackets 



3UC 



4 The frames are made from selected material and by experts 
who thoroughly understand the use of them, and so far as 
human skill can determine the frames are as perfect as it is 
possible to make them. 

4 The gut used in our rackets is the very best that it is possible 
to procure. Our expert makes a special trip abroad every year 
in order that we may get the first and best selection, and we 
believe that our rackets are strung with the choicest gut that 
the world produces, and by stringera who have made a study 
of stringing rackets for years. 

4 We do all it is possible for anyone to do to make perfect 
rackets. Gut, however, is sensitive to atmospheric changes, 
and owners of rackets should take all the precautions they 
possibly can to prevent their being exposed to d&mpness, keep- 
ing the racket in a waterproof cover and in a press. Yet no 
matter how careful a player may be, the natural wear on a 
racket, particularly on dirt courts, often causes the strings to 
give way. The expense of repairing this break (as it occurs 
through no defect of the racket), should be borne by the player.

How to Lay Out a Tennis Court with Marking Tapes 

'THIS illustration shows single court To make a double 
i court, run parallel line 4^ feet distant from line G to 
E, and from F to D, and extend base lines H and I to same. 
The court is 78 feet long and 27 feet wide. It is divided 
across the middle by a net the ends of which are attached 
to two posts, A and B, standing three feet outside of the court 
on either side. The height of the net is three feet 6 inches 
at the posts and three feet in the middle. At each end of 
the court, parallel with the net, and 39 feet from it sre 
drawn the base lines, D E and F G, the ends of which are 
connected by the side lines D F and E G. Half way betwees 
the side lines and parallel with them, is drawn the half eoort 
line I H, dividing the space on either side of the net into two equal parts, the right and left courts. ^On each sidft 
of the net ¬´t a distance of twenty-one feet from i1^ and parallel with it are drawn the service lines. K L and M N.
